Definition and Rulings of 'Itaq (Manumission)
Defines 'Itaq and discusses its legal nature and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal definition of 'itaq, identify its ruling as mandub, and distinguish between Abu Hanifa's view (divisible) and the Sahibayn's view (indivisible) regarding partial manumission.

Core Idea
'Itaq is the establishment of legal power in the slave; it is recommendable (mandub) and divisible according to Abu Hanifa.

This passage introduces the Book of Manumission, defining 'itaq both linguistically and legally. It then clarifies the nature of 'itaq as a recommendable act (not worship), supported by a hadith from 'Ali (ra). The text also presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that 'itaq is the act leading to freedom and thus can be partial, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider it the establishment of freedom itself, making it indivisible. Finally, it enumerates the different legal rulings (obligatory, recommendable, permissible, prohibited) and the essential elements of the manumission contract.

Guided Path
1
Define 'itaq linguistically and legally
Identify the linguistic meaning (power/strength) and the legal definition (establishment of legal power in the slave, enabling testimony and guardianship). Note the connection to divorce as both are cancellations (isqat).
The legal definition is central to the chapter.
2
Analyze the hadith proof
Read the hadith from 'Ali (ra): 'Any Muslim who emancipates a Muslim...' and understand how it indicates the virtue and recommendability (mandub) of 'itaq, not obligation.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but clear in indicating reward.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
Distinguish between Abu Hanifa's view: 'itaq is the act leading to freedom (thus divisible), and the Sahibayn's view: 'itaq is the establishment of freedom (thus indivisible). Recognize the practical consequence for partial manumission.
This ikhtilaf affects cases like 'You are free to the extent of one-third'.
4
Classify the rulings of 'itaq
List the four categories: wajib (for expiation), mandub (for Allah's sake), mubah (without intention), haram (for devil/idol or assuming apostasy). Note that the default is mandub.
The ruling of 'itaq depends on intention and circumstance.
5
Identify the pillar, effect, and causes
State that the rukn is the performative utterance (lafz insha'i). The effect is removal of ownership. The causes include claiming lineage, owning a relative, acknowledging freedom, or entry into dar al-harb.
The causes (asbab) are not the same as conditions; they trigger the possibility of manumission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتاق
أحكام العتاق
كتاب العتاق
أي في بيان أحكامه
وهو قوة شرعيّة تثبت في المحل عند زوال الرّق والملك،
أي العتاق شرعاً
كتاب العتاق: [العتق والعتاق عبارتان عن القوة، والخمر تسمي عتيقاً لزيادة قوتها والكعبة تسمى عتيقاً؛ لقوتها حيث لا يقدر عليها أحد من جهة القهر والغلبة. (مص)] المناسبة بين الطلاق والعتاق أن الطلاق: تخليص الشخص من ذل ملك المتعة وهو أدنى الملكين والإعتاق تخليص الشخص من ذل ملك الرقبة وهو أعلاهما، والعتق والعتاق لغة: قوة مطلقاً . من عتق الطائر إذا قوي على جناحيه فطار عن وكره رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۱/۱] واعلم أن الإسقاطات أنواع ميزت بأسماء لينسب إليها اختصاراً فسمي إسقاط منافع البضع بالطلاق، وملك الرقبة بالإعتاق، وما في الذمة بالإبراء، وملك القصاص بالعفو، ووقع في بعض النسخ الإعتاق من الإفعال، وفي بعضها العتاق فينتظم ملك ذي الرحم المحرم والاستيلاء، والعتاق تصرف مندوب إليه شرعا وإن لم يكن عبادة بدليل جوازه من الكافر، قال علي: "أيما مسلم أعتق مسلماً أعتق الله بكل عضو منه عضواً من النار"؛ ولهذا قالوا: يستحب للرجل أن : يعتق العبد وللمرأة أن تعتق الأمة ليتحقق مقابلة الأعضاء. (مستخلص، فتح) وهو قوة شرعية [ أي إثبات القوة الشرعية التي بها يصير المملوك أهلاً للشهادة والولاية. (ط،عيني)] أي العتاق شرعاً إثبات القوة الشرعية للمملوك؛ لأنه يصير به قادراً على التصرفات الشرعية مثل الولايات والشهادات والسلطنة، وهذا التفسير هو مذهبهما أي العتاق عندهما هو إثبات العتق وعند أبي حنيفة: الإعتاق إثبات الفعل المفضي إلى حصول العتق فلهذا يتجزأ عنده خلافاً لهما، والحرية عبارة عن الخلوص لغةً يقال: أرض حرة لا خراج فيها، وشرعاً عبارة عن خلوص حكمي يظهر في حق الآدمي بانقطاع حق الأغيار عن نفسه، وإثبات هذا الوصف يسمى إعتاقاً وتحريراً. وركنه اللفظ الإنشائي الدال عليه، وصفته: واجب كالإعتاق عن الكفارات، ومندوب كالإعتاق لوجه الله، ومباح كالإعتاق بلا نية وحرام وهو الإعتاق للشيطان أو للصنم أو على ظن أنه يذهب إلى دار الحرب وحكمه زوال الملك وسببه : إما دعوى النسب أو الملك في القريب أو الإقرار بالحرية أو الدخول في دار الحرب. (عيني، فتح) تثبت في المحل أي المملوك، واعلم أن نسخ المتن مختلفة في تعريف الإعتاق ففي بعضها كما في هذا الكتاب، وفي بعضها هو إثبات القوة الشرعية في المملوك، واختاره ملا مسكين ،هذا والعيني نسخة الكتاب، وقوله "عند زوال الرق" ظرف لقوله : "تثبت " كما في هذه النسخة أو لقوله: إثبات" "القوة كما في نسخة ملا مسكين. (محشي) الرق: هو عجز حكمي لا يقدر به على التصرفات والولايات. (مص)
والملك: أي وزوال الملك كأم الولد؛ فإنه وإن زال عنها الرق، لكنه لا يزول عنها ملك المولى. (محشي)
The Book of Manumission. Rulings of Manumission. The Book of Manumission, i.e., concerning the explanation of its rulings. And it is a legal power that is established in the subject at the removal of slavery and ownership. That is, manumission legally. The Book of Manumission: [Manumission and emancipation are expressions for power, and wine is called 'atiq because of its increased potency, and the Ka'bah is called 'atiq because of its strength, as no one can overpower it. (Mabsut)] The connection between divorce and manumission is that divorce is the release of a person from the humiliation of the ownership of enjoyment, which is the lower of the two ownerships, while emancipation is the release of a person from the humiliation of ownership of the person (raqabah), which is the higher of the two. Linguistically, manumission (al-itiq) and emancipation (al-'itaq) mean absolute power, from the phrase 'the bird became free (ataqa) when it gained strength in its wings and flew from its nest. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/321). Know that cancellations (isqatat) are of various types, distinguished by names for brevity: cancellation of the benefits of sexual intercourse is called divorce; cancellation of ownership of the person is called emancipation; cancellation of what is in the dhimmah is called discharge (ibra'); cancellation of the right to retaliation is called pardon (afw). In some manuscripts, 'itaq is from the verbal form if'al, and in others it is 'itaq, which includes ownership of a mahram relative and control. Emancipation is a recommendable (mandub) act in law, even though it is not an act of worship, as evidenced by its permissibility from a disbeliever. 'Ali said: 'Any Muslim who emancipates a Muslim, Allah will emancipate for every limb of his a limb from the Fire.' Hence, they said: It is recommended for a man to emancipate a male slave and for a woman to emancipate a female slave so that the correspondence of limbs is realized. (Mustakhlas, Fath). And it is a legal power [i.e., the establishment of the legal power by which the slave becomes eligible for testimony and guardianship. (Tahawi, 'Ayni)] i.e., legally, emancipation is the establishment of legal power in the slave, because through it he becomes capable of legal actions such as guardianships, testimonies, and authority. This interpretation is the madhhab of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), i.e., emancipation according to them is the establishment of freedom. According to Abu Hanifah, emancipation is the establishment of the act that leads to the attainment of freedom, therefore it is divisible according to him, contrary to the two. Freedom (hurriyyah) linguistically means purity; it is said: 'free land (ard hurrah) meaning no tax on it.' Legally, it means a legal purity that appears in the human being through the cessation of the rights of others over his person. The establishment of this quality is called emancipation (i'taq) and liberation (tahrir). Its pillar (rukn) is the performative utterance (lafz insha'i) indicating it. Its attributes: obligatory, such as emancipation for expiations (kaffarat); recommendable, such as emancipation for the sake of Allah; permissible, such as emancipation without intention; and prohibited, which is emancipation for the devil or for an idol, or on the assumption that he will go to the enemy territory. Its legal effect is the removal of ownership. Its cause (sabab) is either: a claim of lineage, ownership of a relative, acknowledgment of freedom, or entry into enemy territory. ('Ayni, Fath) 'It is established in the subject', i.e., the slave. Know that the manuscripts of the text differ regarding the definition of emancipation. In some, as in this book, it is as given; in others, it is 'the establishment of legal power in the slave', preferred by Mulla Miskin. This is the version of 'Ayni of the book. And his statement 'at the removal of slavery' is an adverbial phrase for his statement 'it is established' as in this manuscript, or for his statement 'establishment of power' as in the manuscript of Mulla Miskin. (Hashiyah) Slavery (al-riqq) is a legal incapacity by which one is unable to perform legal actions and guardianships. (Mabsut) And ownership (al-milk): i.e., along with the removal of ownership, as in the case of umm al-walad; for though slavery is removed from her, the ownership of the master does not cease. (Hashiyah)
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage is the opening of Kitab al-'Itaq (The Book of Manumission) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It begins by defining 'itaq/ 'itaq linguistically and legally. Linguistically, it means power and strength, and the term is used for wine and the Ka'bah due to their strength. The author then draws a parallel between talaq (divorce) and 'itaq: both are forms of cancellation (isqat) but differ in what they cancel—divorce cancels the right to sexual enjoyment, while 'itaq cancels the ownership of the slave's entire person. The text then categorizes cancellations into four types: divorce, emancipation, discharge, and pardon. It notes that 'itaq is a recommended (mandub) act, not a worship, because it is permissible from a disbeliever. A hadith from 'Ali (ra) is cited to show its virtue. The legal definition is given as 'the establishment of legal power in the slave', which enables him to perform legal acts like testimony and guardianship. An intra-Hanafi dispute is mentioned: According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), 'itaq is the establishment of freedom itself, so it cannot be partial. According to Abu Hanifah, 'itaq is the act leading to freedom, so it can be divisible. This difference has practical implications for partial manumission (ta'bid). The text then explains the pillar (rukn) of 'itaq, which is the performative utterance, and classifies its rulings (obligatory, recommended, permissible, prohibited). It states the effect is removal of ownership of the slave, and the causes include: claiming paternity (if the slave is one's child), owning a relative (like a father owning his son), acknowledging the slave's freedom, or the slave entering enemy territory. Finally, it clarifies that the phrase 'at the removal of slavery and ownership' includes both slavery and ownership, giving the example of umm al-walad, where slavery is removed but ownership remains.

Key Terms
العتاق (al-'itāq): Legal power established in the slave upon the removal of slavery and ownership; the act of manumission.
الرق (al-riqq): A legal incapacity that prevents the slave from performing legal actions and guardianships.
الملك (al-milk): Ownership of the master over the slave; in manumission, it is removed along with slavery, but in umm al-walad only slavery is removed while ownership persists until the master's death.
القوة الشرعية (al-quwwah al-shar'iyyah): The legal capacity (ahliyyah) that enables the freed slave to exercise rights like testimony and guardianship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal definition of 'itaq (manumission)?
A) The complete removal of all ownership rights over the slave.
B) The establishment of legal power in the slave, making him eligible for testimony and guardianship.
C) A purely linguistic term meaning power and strength.
D) An act of worship that is obligatory upon every Muslim.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines 'itaq as the establishment of legal power in the slave, making it a recommendable act divisible according to Abu Hanifa, with rulings varying by intention and circumstance.

beginner
8 min
Definition and Legal Ruling of 'Itq
Defines 'itq linguistically and legally, with proof of its recommended status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of 'itq and explain why emancipation is considered a recommended act (mandub) rather than a pure act of worship.

Core Idea
'Itq is a legal power (quwwa shar'iyya) making the slave eligible for legal acts; it is recommended, not compulsory worship.

This lesson opens the book of emancipation by defining 'itq both linguistically and legally. You will learn how emancipation fits within the broader Hanafi framework of legal nullifications (isqat) and understand its recommended nature supported by a hadith of 'Ali (RA).

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic from legal meaning
The word 'itq linguistically means strength (from a bird becoming strong enough to fly). Legally, it is a legal power that gives the slave legal capacity.
The linguistic meaning provides a metaphor for the legal concept.
2
Identify the connection with divorce
Both divorce and emancipation are forms of release from ownership—divorce from owning usufruct, emancipation from owning the person. This categorizes them as types of isqat (nullification).
This helps understand the legal taxonomy.
3
Classify the act's ruling
The text states emancipation is mandub (recommended), not an ibadah (worship), because it is valid from a disbeliever.
This is a key distinction from other acts of worship.
4
Examine the proof
The hadith of 'Ali (RA) promises reward for emancipation. In Hanafi usul, promise of reward indicates istihbab (recommendation), not obligation.
This is the main dalil for the ruling.

The Book of Emancipation (explaining its rulings). And it is a legal power that is established in the subject upon the removal of slavery and ownership, meaning 'itq legally. The Book of Emancipation: [al-'itq and al-'itaq are two expressions for power; wine is called 'atiq due to its increased strength, and the Ka'ba is called 'atiq due to its strength since no one can overpower it by force and domination. (Mabsut)] The connection between divorce and emancipation is that divorce is the release of a person from the humiliation of the ownership of usufruct, which is the lesser of the two ownerships, while emancipation is the release of a person from the humiliation of the ownership of the neck (the slave), which is the higher of the two. Al-'itq and al-'itaq linguistically mean absolute strength, from the bird being 'atiq when it becomes strong on its wings and flies from its nest. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:321) Know that the acts of nullification are of various types distinguished by names for brevity: the nullification of the benefits of sexual intercourse is called divorce, the nullification of ownership of the neck is called emancipation, what is in the dhimmah is called ibra', and the ownership of retaliation is called 'afw. It occurs in some copies that al-i'taq is from al-if'al (active), and in some that al-'itaq includes the ownership of a close relative and appropriation. Emancipation is a recommended (mandub) act legally, even if it is not an act of worship, evidenced by its permissibility from a disbeliever. 'Ali said: "Any Muslim who emancipates a Muslim, Allah will emancipate a limb from the Fire for every limb of him." Hence they said: It is recommended for a man to emancipate a male slave and for a woman to emancipate a female slave so that the correspondence of limbs is realized. (Mukhtalas, Fath) And it is a legal power, meaning the establishment of legal power by which the slave becomes eligible for testimony and guardianship. (Tal, 'Ayni) Meaning, 'itq legally is the establishment of legal power for the slave, because through it he becomes capable of legal dispositions such as guardianship, testimony, and sovereignty. This interpretation is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), i.e., 'itq according to them is the establishment of 'itq; while according to Abu Hanifa, i'taq is the establishment of the act leading to the occurrence of 'itq, so it is divisible according to him, contrary to them. Al-hurriyah is linguistically freedom; it is said: free land with no kharaj on it. Legally, it is a notional freedom manifesting in a person by the cutting off of the rights of others from himself. The establishment of this quality is called i'taq and tahrir. Its pillar is the constitutive utterance indicating it. Its ruling: obligatory (like emancipation from expiations), recommended (like emancipation for the sake of Allah), permissible (like emancipation without intention), and forbidden (like emancipation for the devil or an idol, or on the assumption that the slave will go to dar al-harb). Its effect is the removal of ownership. Its cause is either a claim of lineage, ownership of a relative, acknowledgment of freedom, or entry into dar al-harb. ('Ayni, Fath) 'Established in the place' i.e., the owned slave. Know that the copies of the matn differ in the definition of i'taq: in some as in this book, in some it is 'the establishment of legal power in the slave', chosen by Mulla Miskin. This is the copy of al-'Ayni. And his statement 'upon the removal of slavery' is an adverb for 'is established' in this copy, or for 'establishment of power' in the copy of Mulla Miskin. (Hashiyah) Ar-riqq is a notional incapacity by which one is incapable of legal dispositions and guardianships. (Mabsut) And al-milk, i.e., the removal of ownership, like umm al-walad; for even though slavery is removed from her, the ownership of the master is not removed from her. (Hashiyah)
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage opens Kitab al-'Itq by defining the term both linguistically and legally. It begins by explaining the connection between divorce and emancipation: both are forms of releasing a person from a form of ownership—divorce from the lesser ownership of usufruct, emancipation from the greater ownership of the slave's entire person. The linguistic root of 'itq is strength, as in a bird becoming strong enough to fly. The author then clarifies that the various types of legal release (isqat) are given distinct names: isqat (nullification) of the benefits of marriage is called talaq, of the ownership of the slave's person is called i'taq, of a debt is ibra', and of the right to retaliation is 'afw. The legal definition is then given: 'itq is the establishment of legal power in the slave, making him capable of legal acts like testimony and guardianship. The passage highlights an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa considers i'taq to be the establishment of the act leading to freedom, making it divisible (tajazzu'), while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it to be the establishment of the very quality of freedom, and therefore not divisible. The recommendation (nadb) of emancipation is supported by a hadith of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) about the reward of emancipation. The passage also notes that emancipation is not considered an 'ibadah in the technical sense because it is permissible from a disbeliever. The 'ruling of 'itq' is then categorized according to intention: obligatory if for expiations, recommended if purely for Allah, permissible if without specific intention, and forbidden if for an idol or the devil. The cause (sabab) of emancipation can be a claim of lineage, ownership of a relative, acknowledgment of freedom, or entry into dar al-harb. The effect is the removal of ownership. Finally, the passage explains that 'riqq' is a notional incapacity, and 'milk' (ownership) may persist even after slavery is removed, as in the case of umm al-walad.

Key Terms
عتق ('itq): Legally, the establishment of legal power in a slave, making him capable of legal dispositions.
حرية (hurriyah): Notional freedom; the absence of the rights of others over a person.
رق (riqq): Notional incapacity that prevents a person from legal dispositions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal nature of 'itq (emancipation)?
A) It is a pure act of worship like salah.
B) It is a recommended act that is not technically an 'ibadah.
C) It is always obligatory on every Muslim.
D) It is forbidden if done without intention.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines 'itq as a legal power making the slave capable of legal acts, and its default ruling is recommendation (nadb), not obligation.

The Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on the Divisibility of 'Itq
Explains the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether 'itq is divisible and presents the arguments of both sides.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the divisibility of 'itq and identify the practical consequence of this ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa holds 'itq is divisible because it is an act; the Sahibayn hold it is an indivisible legal quality.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi difference: whether emancipation can be partial. Abu Hanifa considers the act of freeing as divisible, while the Sahibayn view 'itq as a single legal quality. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of legal definitions and their practical effects.

Guided Path
1
State the difference in definition
Abu Hanifa defines i'taq as the act leading to freedom; Sahibayn define 'itq as the establishment of legal power itself.
This difference is not merely semantic; it affects divisibility.
2
Analyze the reasoning
Abu Hanifa derives his view from the linguistic structure (i'taq is an active verb, hence an act). Sahibayn use qiyas to death, which is a single indivisible event.
The qiyas to death is a key argument for the Sahibayn.
3
Apply to the scenario of partial emancipation
If someone says 'half of you is free', Abu Hanifa says half becomes free; Sahibayn say no effect.
This thamarah al-khilaf is the practical difference.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn: 'itq is not divisible.
This is the operative ruling in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتاق (أي في بيان أحكامه) وهو قوة شرعيّة تثبت في المحل عند زوال الرّق والملك، أي العتاق شرعاً كتاب العتاق: [العتق والعتاق عبارتان عن القوة، والخمر تسمي عتيقاً لزيادة قوتها والكعبة تسمى عتيقاً؛ لقوتها حيث لا يقدر عليها أحد من جهة القهر والغلبة. (مص)] المناسبة بين الطلاق والعتاق أن الطلاق: تخليص الشخص من ذل ملك المتعة وهو أدنى الملكين والإعتاق تخليص الشخص من ذل ملك الرقبة وهو أعلاهما، والعتق والعتاق لغة: قوة مطلقاً . من عتق الطائر إذا قوي على جناحيه فطار عن وكره رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۱/۱] واعلم أن الإسقاطات أنواع ميزت بأسماء لينسب إليها اختصاراً فسمي إسقاط منافع البضع بالطلاق، وملك الرقبة بالإعتاق، وما في الذمة بالإبراء، وملك القصاص بالعفو، ووقع في بعض النسخ الإعتاق من الإفعال، وفي بعضها العتاق فينتظم ملك ذي الرحم المحرم والاستيلاء، والعتاق تصرف مندوب إليه شرعا وإن لم يكن عبادة بدليل جوازه من الكافر، قال علي: "أيما مسلم أعتق مسلماً أعتق الله بكل عضو منه عضواً من النار"؛ ولهذا قالوا: يستحب للرجل أن : يعتق العبد وللمرأة أن تعتق الأمة ليتحقق مقابلة الأعضاء. (مستخلص، فتح) وهو قوة شرعية [ أي إثبات القوة الشرعية التي بها يصير المملوك أهلاً للشهادة والولاية. (ط،عيني)] أي العتاق شرعاً إثبات القوة الشرعية للمملوك؛ لأنه يصير به قادراً على التصرفات الشرعية مثل الولايات والشهادات والسلطنة، وهذا التفسير هو مذهبهما أي العتاق عندهما هو إثبات العتق وعند أبي حنيفة: الإعتاق إثبات الفعل المفضي إلى حصول العتق فلهذا يتجزأ عنده خلافاً لهما، والحرية عبارة عن الخلوص لغةً يقال: أرض حرة لا خراج فيها، وشرعاً عبارة عن خلوص حكمي يظهر في حق الآدمي بانقطاع حق الأغيار عن نفسه، وإثبات هذا الوصف يسمى إعتاقاً وتحريراً. وركنه اللفظ الإنشائي الدال عليه، وصفته: واجب كالإعتاق عن الكفارات، ومندوب كالإعتاق لوجه الله، ومباح كالإعتاق بلا نية وحرام وهو الإعتاق للشيطان أو للصنم أو على ظن أنه يذهب إلى دار الحرب وحكمه زوال الملك وسببه : إما دعوى النسب أو الملك في القريب أو الإقرار بالحرية أو الدخول في دار الحرب. (عيني، فتح) تثبت في المحل أي المملوك، واعلم أن نسخ المتن مختلفة في تعريف الإعتاق ففي بعضها كما في هذا الكتاب، وفي بعضها هو إثبات القوة الشرعية في المملوك، واختاره ملا مسكين ،هذا والعيني نسخة الكتاب، وقوله "عند زوال الرق" ظرف لقوله : "تثبت " كما في هذه النسخة أو لقوله: إثبات" "القوة كما في نسخة ملا مسكين. (محشي) الرق: هو عجز حكمي لا يقدر به على التصرفات والولايات. (مص) والملك: أي وزوال الملك كأم الولد؛ فإنه وإن زال عنها الرق، لكنه لا يزول عنها ملك المولى. (محشي)
The Book of Emancipation (explaining its rulings). And it is a legal power that is established in the subject upon the removal of slavery and ownership, meaning 'itq legally. The Book of Emancipation: [al-'itq and al-'itaq are two expressions for power; wine is called 'atiq due to its increased strength, and the Ka'ba is called 'atiq due to its strength since no one can overpower it by force and domination. (Mabsut)] The connection between divorce and emancipation is that divorce is the release of a person from the humiliation of the ownership of usufruct, which is the lesser of the two ownerships, while emancipation is the release of a person from the humiliation of the ownership of the neck (the slave), which is the higher of the two. Al-'itq and al-'itaq linguistically mean absolute strength, from the bird being 'atiq when it becomes strong on its wings and flies from its nest. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:321) Know that the acts of nullification are of various types distinguished by names for brevity: the nullification of the benefits of sexual intercourse is called divorce, the nullification of ownership of the neck is called emancipation, what is in the dhimmah is called ibra', and the ownership of retaliation is called 'afw. It occurs in some copies that al-i'taq is from al-if'al (active), and in some that al-'itaq includes the ownership of a close relative and appropriation. Emancipation is a recommended (mandub) act legally, even if it is not an act of worship, evidenced by its permissibility from a disbeliever. 'Ali said: "Any Muslim who emancipates a Muslim, Allah will emancipate a limb from the Fire for every limb of him." Hence they said: It is recommended for a man to emancipate a male slave and for a woman to emancipate a female slave so that the correspondence of limbs is realized. (Mukhtalas, Fath) And it is a legal power, meaning the establishment of legal power by which the slave becomes eligible for testimony and guardianship. (Tal, 'Ayni) Meaning, 'itq legally is the establishment of legal power for the slave, because through it he becomes capable of legal dispositions such as guardianship, testimony, and sovereignty. This interpretation is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), i.e., 'itq according to them is the establishment of 'itq; while according to Abu Hanifa, i'taq is the establishment of the act leading to the occurrence of 'itq, so it is divisible according to him, contrary to them. Al-hurriyah is linguistically freedom; it is said: free land with no kharaj on it. Legally, it is a notional freedom manifesting in a person by the cutting off of the rights of others from himself. The establishment of this quality is called i'taq and tahrir. Its pillar is the constitutive utterance indicating it. Its ruling: obligatory (like emancipation from expiations), recommended (like emancipation for the sake of Allah), permissible (like emancipation without intention), and forbidden (like emancipation for the devil or an idol, or on the assumption that the slave will go to dar al-harb). Its effect is the removal of ownership. Its cause is either a claim of lineage, ownership of a relative, acknowledgment of freedom, or entry into dar al-harb. ('Ayni, Fath) 'Established in the place' i.e., the owned slave. Know that the copies of the matn differ in the definition of i'taq: in some as in this book, in some it is 'the establishment of legal power in the slave', chosen by Mulla Miskin. This is the copy of al-'Ayni. And his statement 'upon the removal of slavery' is an adverb for 'is established' in this copy, or for 'establishment of power' in the copy of Mulla Miskin. (Hashiyah) Ar-riqq is a notional incapacity by which one is incapable of legal dispositions and guardianships. (Mabsut) And al-milk, i.e., the removal of ownership, like umm al-walad; for even though slavery is removed from her, the ownership of the master is not removed from her. (Hashiyah)
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage opens Kitab al-'Itq by defining the term both linguistically and legally. It begins by explaining the connection between divorce and emancipation: both are forms of releasing a person from a form of ownership—divorce from the lesser ownership of usufruct, emancipation from the greater ownership of the slave's entire person. The linguistic root of 'itq is strength, as in a bird becoming strong enough to fly. The author then clarifies that the various types of legal release (isqat) are given distinct names: isqat (nullification) of the benefits of marriage is called talaq, of the ownership of the slave's person is called i'taq, of a debt is ibra', and of the right to retaliation is 'afw. The legal definition is then given: 'itq is the establishment of legal power in the slave, making him capable of legal acts like testimony and guardianship. The passage highlights an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa considers i'taq to be the establishment of the act leading to freedom, making it divisible (tajazzu'), while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it to be the establishment of the very quality of freedom, and therefore not divisible. The recommendation (nadb) of emancipation is supported by a hadith of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) about the reward of emancipation. The passage also notes that emancipation is not considered an 'ibadah in the technical sense because it is permissible from a disbeliever. The 'ruling of 'itq' is then categorized according to intention: obligatory if for expiations, recommended if purely for Allah, permissible if without specific intention, and forbidden if for an idol or the devil. The cause (sabab) of emancipation can be a claim of lineage, ownership of a relative, acknowledgment of freedom, or entry into dar al-harb. The effect is the removal of ownership. Finally, the passage explains that 'riqq' is a notional incapacity, and 'milk' (ownership) may persist even after slavery is removed, as in the case of umm al-walad.

Key Terms
عتق ('itq): The legal quality of being free; according to Sahibayn, it is the establishment of legal power in the slave, making him capable of legal dispositions.
إعتاق (i'taq): The act of emancipating; according to Abu Hanifa, it is the act that leads to freedom; according to Sahibayn, it is synonymous with 'itq (the establishment of legal power).
تجزئة (tajazzu'): Divisibility; the quality of being divisible into parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the intra-Hanafi dispute on 'itq, what is the practical consequence if someone says 'half of you is free' according to the Sahibayn?
A) Half of the slave becomes free.
B) The slave remains fully enslaved.
C) The slave becomes fully free.
D) It depends on the owner's intention.

Key Takeaway
The Sahibayn's definition of 'itq as an indivisible legal quality is the mufta bihi, meaning partial emancipation is ineffective unless expressed as a conditional promise.

Conditions and Expressions of Valid 'Itq (Emancipation)
Lists conditions for valid emancipation and valid expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid emancipation and distinguish between sarih and kinayah expressions, applying the Hanafi rules to determine validity.

Core Idea
Emancipation requires ownership, a capable owner, and a valid expression; sarih needs no intention, kinayah does.

Emancipation ('itq) is a recommended act in the Hanafi school, but its validity depends on strict conditions regarding the person performing it, the object, and the expression. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq details these conditions and distinguishes between explicit (sarih) and allusive (kinayah) expressions. Understanding this distinction is crucial because it determines whether intention (niyyah) is required for the emancipation to take effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act of emancipation ('itq). Determine its overall hukm: it is sunnah mu'akkadah (recommended), but the passage focuses on conditions for validity, not on recommending the act itself.
The passage does not discuss the ruling of performing emancipation, only its validity conditions.
2
Extract the conditions for validity
List the shurut (conditions) that must be present for emancipation to be valid: emancipator must be free, mukallaf (sane, adult), must own the slave at the time of utterance, must not be in doubt about the occurrence, and the expression must be either sarih or intended kinayah.
Conditions are explicitly listed in the text; note the exclusion of non-mukallaf individuals.
3
Classify the expressions (sarih vs kinayah)
Identify which expressions are sarih (explicit) and thus do not require intention, and which are kinayah (allusive) and require intention. Sarih: 'anta hurr', 'atiq', 'mu'taq', 'muharrar', 'harrartuka', 'a'taqtuka'. Kinayah: 'la malik li 'alayka', 'la 'ilaaqati', etc.
The passage also discusses parts expressing the whole (head, face) as valid, and parts that do not (hand, leg) as invalid.
4
Examine the proof (dalil)
The passage cites the hadith 'La 'itqa fima la yamliku ibn Adam' to prove that emancipation of another's slave is invalid even if later purchased. Analyze thubut (zanni) and dalalah (qati').
The hadith is ahad, but the meaning is clear and decisive.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Identify the dispute between Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn regarding emancipation of a harbi mahram relative in Dar al-Harb. The mufta bihi position is that of the Sahibayn (does not take effect). Also note Abu Yusuf's view on the expression 'your vagina is free' for a female slave.
These ikhtilafat are mentioned in the commentary and have practical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصح من حرّ مكلّف المملوكه بأنت حر أو بما يعبر به عن البدن، وعتيق ومعتق ومحرر وحرّرتك وأعتقتُك نواه أولا، وبلا ملك لي ولا رق ولا سبيل لي عليك أي وأنت محرر إن نواه .. أي لي عليك من حر مكلف: وإن لم يكن عالماً بأنه مملوك، فلا يصح من العبد، ولا من غير العاقل والبالغ، فلو أضاف العتق إلى تلك الحالة بأن قال: أعتقته وأنا صبي . أو مجنون، وجنونه معهود لم يعتق وكذا إذا قال في حال صباه أو جنونه: إذا بلغت أو أفقت فهو حر لم ينعقد؛ لأن قوله غير ملزم وقوله : "لمملوكه" قيد بالمملوك؛ لأنه لا ينفذ العتق في ملك الغير، فإن قال لعبد الغير: أنت حر ثم اشتراه لم يعتق عليه؛ لقوله عليه: "لا عتق فيما لا يملك ابن آدم" . رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۱/۱] وكذا يشترط أن لا يكون شاكاً في ثبوت الإعتاق، فإن كان شاكاً فيه لا يحكم بثبوته، وقيد بالمكلف فخرج به الصبي والمجنون والمعتوه والمدهوش والمبرسم والمغمى عليه والنائم، ولا فرق بين كون المعتق مسلماً أو كافراً في دار الإسلام؛ لأن العتق ليس بعبادة وضعاً؛ ولهذا لم يتوقف على النية، فلو ملك قريباً محرماً حربياً بدار الحرب فإنه لا يعتق عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف الله، ولو ملك قريبه الذمي أو المسلم بدار الحرب عتق بالإجماع، وكذا لو أضاف العتق إلى الملك كإن ملكتك أو على سببه كإن اشتريتك فأنت حر. (فتح) بأنت حر متعلق بقوله يصح أي شرطه أن يكون مضافاً إلى الجملة كقوله: أنت حر. (عيني) يعبر به عن البدن أي يكون مضافاً إلى جزئها يعبر به عن الجملة كقولك: رأسك حر ووجهك حر. (عيني) عن البدن: يعني عن الجملة، و لم يقل: كما في الطلاق أو بجزء شائع؛ للفرق البين بينهما وهو أن العتق مما يتجزأ بخلاف الطلاق والأجزاء التي يعبر به عن الجملة كالرأس والوجه بأن قال: رأسك حر ووجهك حر، ولا يعتق بقوله: يدك حر ورجلك حر؛ لأنه لا يعبر به عن الجملة، وعن أبي يوسف الله: إذا قال لأمته: فرجك حر عتقت، ولو قال لعبده لا يعتق؛ لأن الفرج يعبر به عن الكل في المرأة دون الرجل. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٢١/١] وعتيق ومعتق: أي ويصح أيضاً بقوله: أنت عتيق، أي وأنت معتق. (عيني) وأعتقتك: لأن هذه الألفاظ صريح في الإعتاق؛ لاختصاص استعمالها فيه أو لغلبته. (عيني) نواه أو لا أي يصح العتق بالألفاظ المذكورة من قوله: "أنت حر" إلى "أعقتك" نوى القائل العتق أو لم ينو؛ لأنها صريح في العتق فلو قال أردت به الإخبار الباطل أو أنه حر من العمل لا يصدق قضاء؛ لأنه خلاف الظاهر، ويصدق ديانة؛ لأنه محتمل كلامه. رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۱/۱] وبلا ملك لي: أي ويصح أيضاً بقوله لعبده لا ملك لي عليك (عيني) إن نواه: أي لو نوى بهذه الألفاظ العتق؛ لأنها كنايات فلا يتعين المراد إلا بالنية وهذا بالإجماع إلا في رواية عن أحمد: يقع بلا نية. (عيني)
And it is valid from a free, legally responsible (mukallaf) person regarding his owned slave, by saying 'You are free' or by what expresses the whole body, and (by saying) 'atiq, mu'taq, muharrar, harrartuka, a'taqtuka', whether he intends it or not. And (it is valid) by saying 'there is no ownership for me over you, no slavery, no right over you' intending (emancipation). 'And you are free' if he intends it. 'There is no right over me over you.' From a free, mukallaf person, even if he is unaware that the slave is owned. So it is not valid from a slave, nor from one who is not sane and adult. If he adds the emancipation to that state by saying 'I emancipated him while I was a child or insane' with habitual insanity, it does not take effect. Likewise, if he says during his childhood or insanity: 'if I reach puberty or recover, he is free', it is not valid because his statement is not binding. And his statement 'regarding his owned slave' is a condition of ownership, because emancipation does not take effect in the property of another. If he says to another's slave 'you are free' then buys him, it does not take effect due to his statement: 'There is no emancipation regarding what a son of Adam does not own'. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:321) Likewise, it is a condition that he is not in doubt about the occurrence of emancipation, for if he is in doubt, it is not ruled to have occurred. The condition 'mukallaf' excludes the child, madman, imbecile, bewildered, delirious, unconscious, and sleeping person. There is no difference between the emancipator being Muslim or disbeliever in Dar al-Islam, because emancipation is not an act of worship in its nature; hence it does not depend on intention. If he owns a mahram relative who is a harbi in Dar al-Harb, it does not take effect according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf (in one narration). If he owns his dhimmi or Muslim relative in Dar al-Harb, it takes effect by consensus. Likewise, if he adds emancipation to ownership, like 'if I own you', or to its cause, like 'if I buy you, you are free'. (Fath) 'By saying you are free' is connected to his statement 'it is valid', meaning its condition is that it is attached to a sentence like 'you are free'. ('Ayni) 'Or by what expresses the body', meaning it is attached to a part that expresses the whole, like saying 'your head is free, your face is free'. ('Ayni) 'About the body' means about the whole. And he did not say 'as in divorce' or 'by a common part' due to the clear difference: that emancipation is divisible (tajazzu') unlike divorce. Parts that express the whole are like the head and face. If he says 'your hand is free, your leg is free', it does not take effect because they do not express the whole. From Abu Yusuf: if he says to his female slave 'your vagina is free', she becomes free; but if he says to his male slave, he does not become free, because the vagina expresses the whole in a woman but not in a man. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:321) 'And atiq and mu'taq': it is also valid by saying 'you are atiq' and 'you are mu'taq'. ('Ayni) 'And a'taqtuka': because these words are explicit (sarih) for emancipation due to their specific usage or predominance. ('Ayni) 'Whether he intends it or not': emancipation is valid with the mentioned words from 'you are free' to 'a'taqtuka' whether the speaker intended emancipation or not, because they are explicit. If he says 'I meant by it false information or that he is free from work', it is not accepted judicially because it contradicts the apparent meaning, but it is accepted religiously (diyaanatan) because his speech could mean that. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:321) 'And by saying 'there is no ownership for me': it is also valid by saying to his slave 'there is no ownership for me over you' if he intends it. ('Ayni) 'If he intends it': i.e., if he intends these words for emancipation, because they are kinayah (allusive) so the intended meaning is only determined by intention. This is by consensus, except in a narration from Ahmad that it occurs without intention. ('Ayni)
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage details the conditions for a valid emancipation and the expressions that effect it. It begins by stating that emancipation is valid only from a free (not a slave), mukallaf (sane, adult) person regarding his own slave. The emancipator need not know the slave is owned; ignorance does not affect validity. Invalid are emancipation by a slave (since he lacks authority), by a minor, insane, or unconscious person. If someone says 'I emancipated him while I was a child' or conditions emancipation on a future event while in a state of childhood or insanity, it is not effective because the statement is not binding. Ownership is a condition: emancipation of another's slave is invalid, even if later purchased, due to the hadith 'No emancipation regarding what a son of Adam does not own.' Doubt about the occurrence of emancipation prevents its legal effect. The emancipator can be Muslim or disbeliever in Dar al-Islam, since emancipation is not an act of worship. Special rules apply in Dar al-Harb: if a harbi owns a mahram relative, it does not take effect according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (contrary to a narration from Abu Yusuf), but if the relative is a dhimmi or Muslim, it takes effect by consensus. Adding emancipation to the cause (e.g., 'if I buy you, you are free') is valid. The passage then discusses valid expressions: explicit (sarih) expressions like 'you are free', 'you are atiq', 'you are mu'taq', 'you are muharrar', 'I have emancipated you' effect emancipation regardless of intention. Expressions like 'no ownership for me over you' are kinayah and require intention. Parts that express the whole, like 'your head is free' or 'your face is free', also effect emancipation, but parts that do not express the whole, like 'your hand is free', do not. There is a detail from Abu Yusuf that saying 'your vagina is free' to a female slave emancipates her, but not to a male slave, because that part expresses the whole for women. Finally, sarih expressions are effective judicially even if the speaker claims a different intention, but diyaanatan (in matters between him and Allah) his claim is accepted if plausible. Kinayah expressions depend on intention by consensus (except a narration from Ahmad).

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): Words that are exclusive to emancipation and need no intention; they effect emancipation even if the speaker claims otherwise.
كناية (kinayah): Words that can imply emancipation but need intention; they only effect emancipation if the speaker intended that meaning.
عتق ('itq): Emancipation; the act of freeing a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following expressions for emancipation is considered sarih (explicit) and does not require intention?
A) 'There is no ownership for me over you.'
B) 'You are free.'
C) 'I have no right over you.'
D) 'Your hand is free.'

Key Takeaway
Valid emancipation requires ownership, a capable person, and a proper expression; sarih words need no intention, kinayah words do.

Conditions for Valid Manumission and the Sarih/Kinayah Distinction
Conditions for manumission and the sarih/kinayah distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three conditions for a valid manumission and distinguish between sarih and kinayah phrases regarding the requirement of intention.

Core Idea
Manumission by explicit words needs no intention – the phrase itself effects the freeing.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq opens the Book of Manumission by setting the essential conditions. The Hanafi school classifies manumission as a legal disposition, not an act of worship, which affects the role of intention. Understanding these conditions is crucial for determining when a manumission is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the manumitter's required qualities
The text states manumission is valid from a free, legally accountable person. This means the manumitter must be free (not a slave) and must be sane and adult (mukallaf).
2
Identify the required relationship to the slave
The manumitter must own the slave at the time of the act. The passage explicitly says 'his owned slave' and cites the hadith to reinforce ownership.
3
Distinguish between sarih and kinayah expressions
Sarih phrases like 'anta hurr' require no intention; kinayah phrases like 'la mulka li' require intention to effect manumission.
This distinction is a hallmark of Hanafi fiqh.
4
Note exceptions for minors and insane persons
Manumission from a child or insane person is invalid. If the manumission is attributed to a past state of incapacity, it does not take effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتاق
أحكام العتاق ويصح من حرّ مكلّف المملوكه بأنت حر أو بما يعبر به عن البدن، وعتيق ومعتق ومحرر وحرّرتك وأعتقتُك نواه أولا، وبلا ملك لي ولا رق ولا سبيل لي عليك أي وأنت محرر إن نواه .. أي لي عليك من حر مكلف: وإن لم يكن عالماً بأنه مملوك، فلا يصح من العبد، ولا من غير العاقل والبالغ، فلو أضاف العتق إلى تلك الحالة بأن قال: أعتقته وأنا صبي . أو مجنون، وجنونه معهود لم يعتق وكذا إذا قال في حال صباه أو جنونه: إذا بلغت أو أفقت فهو حر لم ينعقد؛ لأن قوله غير ملزم وقوله : "لمملوكه" قيد بالمملوك؛ لأنه لا ينفذ العتق في ملك الغير، فإن قال لعبد الغير: أنت حر ثم اشتراه لم يعتق عليه؛ لقوله عليه: "لا عتق فيما لا يملك ابن آدم" . رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۱/۱]
The Book of Manumission. Rulings of manumission: It is valid from a free, legally accountable [person] regarding his owned slave, by [saying] 'You are free' or by what expresses the whole body, and ['atiq], ['mu'taq], ['muharrar], ['harrartuka], ['a'taqtuka], whether he intended [it] or not, and [by] 'no ownership have I over you', 'no right', 'no authority', i.e., 'you are freed if he intended it'... i.e., 'I have [no authority] over you'. From a free, accountable person: even if he was not aware that he is owned. So it is not valid from a slave, nor from one who is not sane and adult. If he attaches manumission to that state by saying: 'I freed him while I was a child' or 'insane', and his insanity was habitual, he is not freed. Likewise if he said during his childhood or insanity: 'If I reach maturity or recover sanity, he is free' – it is not binding because his statement is not binding. And His statement: 'his owned slave' is conditioned by ownership; because manumission does not take effect in the property of another. If he says to another's slave: 'You are free' then buys him, it does not become effective for him due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'There is no manumission in what the son of Adam does not own.' [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:321]
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It opens the Book of Manumission ('Itq) by stating the essential conditions for a valid manumission: the manumitter must be free (hurr), legally accountable (mukallaf – sane and adult), and must own the slave (mamluk). The manumission is valid using explicit phrases like 'anta hurr' (you are free) or any phrase that indicates the whole body (e.g., 'ra'suka hurr' – your head is free). The list also includes other explicit terms: 'atiq, mu'taq, muharrar, harrartuka, a'taqtuka. These are so explicit that no intention (niyyah) is required; they are sarih (clear). Kinayah phrases like 'la mulka li' (no ownership have I) require intention. The commentary then elaborates: if the manumitter was not aware the slave was owned, it still counts. Manumission is invalid from a slave (a slave cannot own slaves) and from a minor or insane person. If the act is attributed to a past state of incapacity, it does not take effect. The key proof is the hadith 'La 'itqa fi ma la yamliku ibn Adam' – there is no manumission in what one does not own. This establishes the condition of ownership. The passage follows Abu Hanifa's principle that manumission is a disposition of ownership, not a pure worship, so it does not require intention for explicit phrases.

Key Terms
عتق ('itq): Manumission: the act of freeing a slave, removing the bond of ownership.
حر مكلف (hurr mukallaf): A free person who is legally accountable: sane, adult, and not in a state of ihram or other impediment.
صريح (sarih): An explicit expression that clearly indicates manumission without need for intention.
كناية (kinayah): An indirect expression that requires intention to effect manumission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT a condition for the validity of manumission?
A) The manumitter must be free.
B) The manumitter must be legally accountable (mukallaf).
C) The manumitter must intend to free the slave when using explicit words.
D) The slave must be owned by the manumitter.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, explicit manumission phrases are effective without intention, provided the conditions of freedom, accountability, and ownership are met.

The Hadith Proof for Ownership as a Condition for Manumission
Hadith proof for ownership condition and its application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the hadith 'La 'itqa fi ma la yamliku ibn Adam' establishes ownership as a condition for manumission and apply it to the case of buying a slave after declaring freedom.

Core Idea
One cannot free a slave one does not own, even if later purchased.

This lesson focuses on the hadith proof cited in the passage. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'There is no manumission in what the son of Adam does not own.' This hadith is the foundation for the ownership condition. The passage applies it to a specific scenario: if someone says to another's slave 'You are free' and then buys him, the manumission does not take effect.

Guided Path
1
Recall the hadith text
The hadith is 'لا عتق فيما لا يملك ابن آدم'. It is a well-known hadith from Sunan al-Tirmidhi.
2
Identify the dalil type
This is a hadith ahad (single-transmitter), so it is zanni in thubut, but Hanafis consider its meaning (dalala) to be qati (certain) because it is unambiguous.
3
Explain the wajh al-dalalah
The negation 'la' in 'la 'itqa' is a negation of validity, meaning that manumission is not legally effective without ownership. Ownership is thus a necessary condition.
4
Apply the rule to the passage's example
If someone says to a slave owned by another 'You are free', and later buys him, the manumission is not valid because at the time of the declaration, the speaker did not own the slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If he attaches manumission to that state by saying: 'I freed him while I was a child' or 'insane', and his insanity was habitual, he is not freed. Likewise if he said during his childhood or insanity: 'If I reach maturity or recover sanity, he is free' – it is not binding because his statement is not binding. And His statement: 'his owned slave' is conditioned by ownership; because manumission does not take effect in the property of another. If he says to another's slave: 'You are free' then buys him, it does not become effective for him due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'There is no manumission in what the son of Adam does not own.' [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:321]

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. Kinayah Phrases Require Intention for Manumission
Ruling statement on kinayah phrases for manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify kinayah expressions for manumission and state the requirement of intention for their validity according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Kinayah expressions like 'la mulka li' require intention (niyyah) to effect manumission; without it, they are not binding.

In Hanafi fiqh, manumission ('itq) can be effected by clear (sarih) or indirect (kinayah) expressions. While sarih words like 'anta hurr' operate automatically, kinayah phrases depend on the speaker's intention. This lesson examines a key kinayah phrase and the rule that intention is necessary, a point on which the Hanafi school holds a consensus apart from a minority report.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
The act is manumission ('itq) via an indirect expression. This is a unilateral disposition (tamlik) that requires the master's declaration.
Manumission is classified as a contract of liberation (itq) in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Determine the ruling and its condition
The ruling is that manumission by kinayah is sahih (valid) ONLY if the speaker intended to free the slave. Without intention, the expression has no legal effect.
The phrase 'la mulka li' is kinayah, not sarih, so intention is a sine qua non.
3
Distinguish sarih from kinayah
Sarih words (e.g., 'anta hurr') always effect manumission regardless of intention. Kinayah words (e.g., 'la mulka li') are ambiguous and require explicit intention.
This distinction is crucial in practice: the same utterance may or may not free the slave depending on the speaker's inner state.
4
Note the cross-madhhab exception
The Hanafi position (and the majority) is that kinayah requires intention. A narration from Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal holds that such phrases effect manumission even without intention. This ikhtilaf is recorded but the Hanafi school sticks to the majority view.
The consensus (ijma') among the Companions and early scholars supports the requirement of intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبلا ملك لي: أي ويصح أيضاً بقوله لعبده لا ملك لي عليك (عيني) إن نواه: أي لو نوى بهذه الألفاظ العتق؛ لأنها كنايات فلا يتعين المراد إلا بالنية وهذا بالإجماع إلا في رواية عن أحمد: يقع بلا نية. (عيني)
And 'no ownership have I over you': i.e., it is also valid by his saying to his slave 'I have no ownership over you' (Ayni) 'if he intended it': i.e., if he intended manumission by these expressions, because they are kinayah (indirect), so the meaning is not determined except by intention, and this is by consensus except in a narration from Ahmad: it occurs without intention. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on kinayah (indirect) phrases. The matn had included phrases like 'la mulka li' (no ownership have I) and 'la raqa li' (no right) as valid for manumission, but the commentary clarifies they are kinayah, not sarih. Therefore, they only effect manumission if the speaker intends ('in nawahu') to free the slave. Without intention, such statements are not binding. The text notes that this rule is by consensus (ijma') among the companions and early scholars, except for a narration from Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal that they cause manumission even without intention. The Hanafi position aligns with the majority: kinayah requires niyyah. This is a key distinction between sarih (explicit) and kinayah (implicit) expressions.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An indirect expression that requires intention to effect manumission.
صريح (sarih): An explicit expression that effects manumission without need for intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master says to his slave 'I have no ownership over you' (la mulka li 'alayka). According to the Hanafi school, when does this cause manumission?
A) Immediately, because it is a clear statement.
B) Only if the master intends to free the slave.
C) Only if the slave accepts the manumission.
D) Never, because it is not a valid phrase.

Key Takeaway
Kinayah phrases require intention to be effective; this is the Hanafi position based on consensus, distinguishing them from sarih expressions that operate automatically.

Divorce Words Do Not Cause Manumission
Ruling that talaq words do not effect 'itq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafis hold that divorce words do not effect manumission and evaluate the Shafi'i/Hanbali counter-argument.

Core Idea
Divorce words are for marriage, not ownership — a word for a lesser right cannot cancel a greater right.

This passage tackles a critical inter-madhhab dispute: can a master free his slave by uttering divorce words, intending manumission? The Hanafi school says no, based on a precise legal analogy (ta'lil) distinguishing two types of ownership. The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools say yes, because intention matters. This dispute reveals deep principles about legal language and intention in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What exactly does the Hanafi school hold regarding using divorce words for manumission?
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school(s) object and on what grounds?
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What is their dalil (evidence) for the permissibility?
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut the opponent's argument?
Focus on the distinction between milk al-nikah and milk al-yamin.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in rulings between the schools?
E.g., if a master says 'anti mutallaqa' intending freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وألفاظ الطلاق: [ أي وكذا لا يعتق بألفاظ الطلاق صريحاً أو كناية وإن نوى (ط)] أي لا يقع العتق بألفاظ الطلاق مثل أن يقول: طلقتك أو أنت مطلق أو قال لأمته أنت مطلقة أو أنت بائن لا يعتق، وقال الشافعي وأحمد: يقع : بألفاظ الطلاق إذا نوى العتق، قلنا: إن هذه الألفاظ وضعت لإزالة أدنى الملكين، وهو ملك النكاح، فلا يكون مزيلاً لأعلىهما، وهو ملك اليمين، وكذا الخلاف في سائر كنايات الطلاق. رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۲/۱] (مستخلص)
And the words of divorce: [i.e., likewise he is not freed by words of divorce, whether explicit or implicit, even if he intended (T)] i.e., manumission does not occur by words of divorce such as 'I divorce you' or 'you are divorced', or if he says to his female slave 'you are divorced' or 'you are bain' – he is not freed. And al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said: it occurs by words of divorce if he intended manumission. We said: these words were placed for the removal of the lesser of the two ownerships, which is the ownership of marriage, so they cannot remove the higher one, which is ownership of the right hand. And similarly, the difference applies to all kinayah of divorce. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:322] (abstracted)
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. It addresses whether words of divorce (talaq) can be used to free a slave if the speaker intends manumission. The Hanafi school says no: words like 'tallaqtuki' (I divorce you) or 'anti mutallaqa' (you are divorced) do not free a female slave, even if the master intends manumission. The reason (ta'lil) is that these words are legislated to dissolve a lesser form of ownership: the marriage contract (nikah). Manumission deals with the higher form: ownership of the right hand (milk al-yamin). A word designed for a lesser right cannot affect a greater right. The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools hold that if the master intends manumission, the word 'divorce' can free the slave because it is a valid declaration of dissolution. The Hanafi response is that the specific wording matters: 'divorce' is specific to marriage; using it for 'itq would be a metaphorical use not recognized in law. This dispute also extends to all kinayah (indirect) expressions of divorce – the Hanafis do not accept them for manumission even with intention, while the other schools do.

Key Terms
ملك النكاح (milk al-nikah): Ownership of marriage – the right to enjoy a spouse, which is a lesser ownership.
ملك اليمين (milk al-yamin): Ownership of the right hand – full ownership over a slave, a greater right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a master says to his female slave 'you are divorced' (anti mutallaqa) intending to free her, what is the ruling?
A) She is freed because the intention is clear.
B) She is not freed because divorce words are for marriage, not manumission.
C) She is freed only if the master also says 'you are free'.
D) She is considered a mukatabah.

Key Takeaway
Divorce words are designed for marriage (lesser ownership), so they cannot effect manumission (greater ownership) even with intention.

Rulings on Implicit and Explicit Emancipation Phrases
Commentary on the effect of two emancipation phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between implicit and explicit emancipation phrases and state their respective rulings according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Implicit phrases require intention; explicit phrases effect immediate emancipation.

In Hanafi fiqh, the words used by a master to declare a slave's freedom determine whether the emancipation takes effect immediately or depends on the master's intention. This lesson examines two common phrases: 'anta mithl al-ḥurr' (you are like a free person) and 'mā anta illā ḥurr' (you are nothing but free). Understanding the distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rulings of emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase
Determine whether the master's statement is implicit (kinayah) or explicit (sarih). The first phrase 'anta mithl al-ḥurr' is implicit; the second 'mā anta illā ḥurr' is explicit.
Implicit phrases allow for multiple interpretations; explicit phrases have a single clear meaning.
2
Check for intention (niyyah)
For implicit phrases, the master must have intended emancipation for it to take effect. For explicit phrases, the statement itself is sufficient and intention is not required.
The Hanafi school considers explicit phrases as definitive, relying on the strength of the wording.
3
Apply the ruling
If the phrase is implicit and no intention is proven, the slave does not become free. If the phrase is explicit, emancipation is immediate regardless of intent.
The basis of the distinction is the degree of certainty (qat') in the meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنت مثل الحر [ أي وكذا لا يعتق بقوله لعبده : أنت مثل الحر. (عيني)] لأنه أثبت المماثلة بينهما وهي قد تكون عامة وقد تكون خاصة فلا يعتق بلا نية الشك (عيني) وعتق إلخ أي العبد بقوله لعبده: "ما أنت إلا - بالحصر؛ لأن فيه إثبات الحرية بأبلغ الوجوه. (عيني)
And you are like the free [meaning: likewise, he does not become free by his saying to his slave: 'You are like the free,' (Ayni) because he affirmed similarity between them, and it may be general or specific, so he does not become free without intention of doubt (Ayni). And emancipation, etc., meaning the slave by his saying to his slave: 'You are nothing but free' – by restriction, because it contains affirmation of freedom in the strongest manner. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two phrases used for emancipation. The first phrase, 'anta mithl al-ḥurr' (you are like a free person), does not effect emancipation unless the speaker intended it, because similarity can be in many aspects and is not definitive. The second phrase, 'mā anta illā ḥurr' (you are nothing but free), does effect emancipation immediately because it is a restrictive statement that affirms freedom in the strongest way, leaving no room for doubt. The commentator 'Ayni explains the reasoning: the first phrase establishes resemblance which may apply to some attributes or all, so it requires intention; the second phrase uses the restrictive 'illā' (except/only), which is an explicit assertion of freedom.

Key Terms
أنت مثل الحر (anta mithl al-ḥurr): A phrase said to a slave meaning 'you are like a free person'; considered implicit and does not effect emancipation without intention.
ما أنت إلا حر (mā anta illā ḥurr): A phrase said to a slave meaning 'you are nothing but free'; considered explicit and effects emancipation immediately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a master says to his slave 'anta mithl al-ḥurr' without any intention to emancipate?
A) The slave is immediately free
B) The slave does not become free
C) The slave becomes free only if the master repeats it
D) The slave becomes free but must pay compensation

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, implicit emancipation phrases require intention; explicit phrases using restriction (hasr) effect immediate freedom.

Emancipation by Owning a Mahram Relative
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on emancipation by owning a mahram relative, identify the proof from the hadith, and distinguish the Hanafi position from those of al-Shafi'i and Malik.

Core Idea
Owning a mahram relative automatically frees them: kinship overrides ownership.

This lesson delves into a key Hanafi ruling: when a person comes to own a close blood relative who is mahram (unmarriageable), that relative becomes free without any action. We will examine the definition of 'dhu rahm mahram', the prophetic proof, and how the Hanafi madhhab differs from the Shafi'i and Maliki schools on the scope of this emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is owning a dhu rahm mahram relative. The ruling is immediate emancipation – the slave becomes free automatically.
2
Define 'dhu rahm mahram' in Hanafi thought
The Hanafi definition: any two persons who trace back to a common ancestor, such as brothers, or uncle and nephew. This is broader than other schools.
The commentator al-Ayni clarifies: one may be through an intermediary, the other directly.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith narrated from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'Whoever owns a dhu rahm mahram relative from him, he is free.' Reported by Abu Dawud and others.
Also reported from Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, and many Tabi'in.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'fa-huwa ḥurr' (he is free) is a declarative statement of immediate effect. In Hanafi usul, such a clear text (nass) indicates obligation or automatic consequence.
The dalil is zanni al-thubut (ahad) but qati' al-dalalah (clear meaning).
5
Note the inter-madhhab dispute
Al-Shafi'i limits emancipation to direct lineage (parents and children). Malik includes siblings but not uncles/aunts. The Hanafi school includes all dhu rahm mahram relatives.
The ikhtilaf is over the definition of 'mahram' and the scope of the hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبملك قريب محرم [ أراد به ذا الرحم المحرم وهو كل شخصين يدليان إلى أصل واحد كالأخوين أو أحدهما بواسطة والآخر بغير واسطة كالعم وابن الأخ (عيني)] أي إذا ملك الرجل ذا رحم محرم منه عتق عليه، وعند الشافعي الله لا يعتق إلا في قرابة الولاد، وذلك مثل الوالدين والمولودين وعند مالك: يعتق فيه وفي قرابة الإخوة والأخوات فقط، ولنا قوله علي: من ملك ذا رحم محرم منه فهو حر"، رواه أبو داود وغيره، وروي عن عمر وابن مسعود ما مثله، كثير وعن التابعين كذلك، ولا يعتق ذو رحم غير محرم كبني الأعمام والأخوال وبني ا والخالات، ولا محرم غير رحم كالمحرمات الصهرية والرضاع إجماعاً.
And by ownership of a close relative [meaning dhu rahm mahram, which is any two persons who trace back to one origin like two brothers, or one through an intermediary and the other without, like the paternal uncle and the brother's son (Ayni)] i.e., if a man owns a dhu rahm mahram relative from him, he becomes free upon him. According to al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, [the slave] does not become free except in the kinship of birth, such as parents and children. According to Malik, he becomes free in that and in the kinship of siblings only. And our proof is the saying of Ali, may Allah be pleased with him: 'Whoever owns a dhu rahm mahram relative from him, he is free.' Reported by Abu Dawud and others. And similar has been reported from Umar and Ibn Mas'ud, many, and from the Tabi'in likewise. And a dhu rahm who is not mahram, like the sons of paternal uncles and maternal uncles and the sons of maternal aunts, does not become free. And a mahram who is not a dhu rahm, like those made mahram by marriage or breastfeeding, does not become free by consensus.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on emancipation through ownership of a mahram relative. The Hanafi school holds that if a person owns a relative who is both a blood relative (dhu rahm) and mahram (unmarriageable), that slave automatically becomes free. The commentator defines dhu rahm mahram as two persons who trace to a common ancestor, like brothers, or one through an intermediate and the other directly (e.g., uncle and nephew). The proof is a prophetic hadith reported by Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'Whoever owns a dhu rahm mahram relative, he is free.' This is also supported by reports from Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, and the Tabi'in. The passage contrasts the Hanafi position with Imam al-Shafi'i, who limits emancipation to direct lineage (parents and children), and Imam Malik, who includes siblings. The text then specifies that the ruling does not apply to non-mahram relatives (e.g., cousins) nor to mahram relationships established by marriage or fosterage (suhriyya or rada'), by consensus.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahm mahram): A blood relative who is unmarriageable (mahram) due to consanguinity, such as parents, children, siblings, uncles, aunts, and their descendants in the direct line.
قرابة الولاد (qarabat al-wilad): Kinship through birth, i.e., direct lineage (parents and children), as opposed to collateral relatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following slaves becomes free automatically when owned by a Muslim man?
A) His maternal uncle's son (cousin)
B) His own son from a slave woman
C) His wife's brother (brother-in-law)
D) A foster brother from breastfeeding

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives automatic freedom to any owned mahram blood relative, supported by a clear hadith and distinguished from other madhhabs by a broader definition.

Emancipation by Minors, the Insane, and in Dar al-Harb
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dar al-harb emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on emancipation by a minor/insane owner in dar al-islam and explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding emancipation in dar al-harb.

Core Idea
In dar al-islam, a minor or insane owner frees a mahram relative automatically; in dar al-harb, physical control matters.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq first affirms that a child or insane person can validly emancipate a mahram relative based on a general hadith, regardless of the owner's faith, as long as it occurs in dar al-islam. It then expands to an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning emancipation in dar al-harb, where Abu Hanifa differs from his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this dispute clarifies how ownership, physical control, and location interact in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Locate the general rule: a minor or insane owner emancipates his mahram relative in dar al-islam. Note that the text says 'due to the generality of the text we narrated,' referring to the hadith 'Whoever owns a dhu rahm mahram relative, he is free.'
The ruling applies whether the owner is Muslim or non-Muslim in dar al-islam.
2
Recognize the expansion to dar al-harb
The text then restricts the ruling to dar al-islam. In dar al-harb, if a harbi owns his relative, or a Muslim owns his relative, or any owner explicitly emancipates a slave, the Sahibayn say it is not effective, while Abu Hanifa says it is effective.
The phrase 'خلافاً لأبي يوسف' in the text is likely a scribal error; the correct opponent is Abu Hanifa.
3
Analyze the reasoning of each side
Abu Hanifa argues that full ownership (malik al-raqabah) gives the right to remove ownership by emancipation. The Sahibayn argue that in dar al-harb, the owner has actual physical control (yad and qahr), so the slave is not truly free until the owner releases that control.
The Sahibayn's view is based on the reality of power in dar al-harb.
4
Note the consensus exception
If the slave is Muslim or dhimmi, all agree that emancipation is effective, because such persons cannot be lawfully enslaved by conquest.
This shows that the ruling depends on the slave's legal status.
5
Determine the operative position
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., Al-Hidayah) adopt the Sahibayn's view as the mufta bihi (operative position) for fatwa.
Thus, in practice, emancipation in dar al-harb is invalid unless the owner relinquishes physical custody.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المالك صبياً إلخ: لإطلاق النص الذي رويناه، سواء كان المالك مسلماً أو كافراً في دارنا، وإنما قيدنا به؛ لأن الحربي لو ملك قريبه في دار الحرب لا يعتق عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف الله، وكذا المسلم لو ملك قريبه في دار الحرب لا يعتق، وكذا لو أعتق الحربي أو المسلم عبداً في دار الحرب لم يعتق عندهما، ويعتق عنده، هو يقول: إنه مالك رقبة فيملك إزالته بالعتق وهما يقولان إنه معتق بلسانه مسترق بيده؛ لأنه تحت يده وقهره حتى لو خلى سبيله وأزال يده عنه عتق، وإن كان العبد مسلماً أو ذمياً عتق بالإجماع؛ لأنهما ليسا بمحل الاسترقاق بالاستيلاء.
The owner being a child, etc.: due to the generality of the text we narrated, whether the owner is Muslim or disbeliever in our land. We only restricted it [to our land] because if a warring disbeliever (harbi) owns his relative in the land of war (dar al-harb), he does not become free according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf (but actually it is 'contrary to Abu Hanifa'? The text says 'خلافاً لأبي يوسف' but likely a typo; it should be 'contrary to Abu Hanifa'? Actually, the OCR says 'لأبي يوسف' but the context suggests it's an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa differs from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The translation follows the text: 'contrary to Abu Yusuf', but that may be an OCR error; however, we translate as is. Also, similarly, if a Muslim owns his relative in dar al-harb, he does not become free. Similarly, if a harbi or Muslim emancipates a slave in dar al-harb, it is not valid according to the two (Sahibayn), but it is valid according to him (Abu Hanifa). He (Abu Hanifa) says: he is the owner of the slave, so he has the power to remove ownership by emancipation. They (Sahibayn) say: he is an emancipator with his tongue but an enslaver with his hand, because the slave is under his hand and power, so if he releases him and removes his hand from him, he becomes free. And if the slave is Muslim or dhimmi, he becomes free by consensus, because they are not subject to enslavement by conquest.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of emancipation by a minor, insane person, or someone in dar al-harb. The text first states that a child or insane person can validly emancipate his mahram relative based on the general hadith, regardless of his faith, as long as it occurs in dar al-islam. Then it presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding dar al-harb. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), if a harbi (warring disbeliever) owns his relative in dar al-harb, the relative does not become free. Similarly, a Muslim owning a relative in dar al-harb also does not lead to emancipation, and the same applies if a harbi or Muslim explicitly emancipates a slave in dar al-harb. However, Abu Hanifa holds that such emancipation is valid in all these cases. His reasoning: the owner has full ownership of the slave, so he can remove it by emancipation. The Sahibayn argue that the owner has actual physical control (yad and qahr) over the slave, and as long as he maintains that control, the slave is effectively still a slave; emancipation only becomes effective if the owner releases physical control. But if the slave is Muslim or dhimmi, all agree that emancipation is effective, because such persons cannot be lawfully enslaved by conquest, so the owner's control does not affect their status.

Key Terms
دار الحرب (dār al-ḥarb): Territory under non-Muslim rule where Islamic law is not enforced; legal rulings may differ from dar al-islam.
ذو رحم محرم (dhū raḥm maḥram): A relative who is within the prohibited degrees of marriage (mahram), such as a parent, sibling, or child.
يد وقهر (yad wa qahr): Physical control and power; the actual ability to hold and dominate a slave, as opposed to mere legal ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what is the status of a slave owned by a Muslim in dar al-harb if the owner says 'You are free'?
A) The slave becomes free immediately
B) The slave does not become free until the owner releases physical control
C) The slave becomes free only if he is Muslim
D) The slave becomes free after leaving dar al-harb

Key Takeaway
In dar al-islam, minor/insane owners automatically free mahram relatives; in dar al-harb, the mufta bihi requires release of physical control for emancipation to take effect.
