Emancipation by a Drunk Person: Hanafi Validity and Distinction
Explains validity of emancipation by a drunk person with a distinction based on cause of intoxication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on emancipation by a drunk person and distinguish between the effects of haram and permissible intoxication.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a drunk person's emancipation is valid if intoxication is from haram, because the essential elements are present.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the validity of emancipation (itq) by a drunk person. The Hanafi school makes a nuanced distinction based on the cause of intoxication. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the difference between haram and permissible intoxicants and their legal effect on the person's capacity. The passage also notes the disagreement of the other three major schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Emancipation (itq) by a drunk person. The Hanafi ruling: it is valid (sahih) in certain conditions.
The text states: 'it is valid if the emancipator is drunk from something forbidden.'
2
Distinguish the cause of intoxication
The ruling depends on whether the intoxication is from a haram (forbidden) source or a permissible (mubah) source. If from haram, the act is valid; if from permissible (e.g., medicinal necessity), the person is like unconscious and all legal acts are invalid.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The reason for validity: the pillar (rukn) of emancipation is present. It came from a qualified person (the master) and was directed to its proper object (the owned slave). Intoxication from haram does not remove the person's eligibility (ahliyyat al-ada') completely.
The text says: 'the reason is that the pillar came from one qualified, added to its proper place.'
4
Note the inter-madhhab disagreement
The three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that any emancipation by a drunk person is invalid, regardless of the cause of intoxication. They consider the drunk person as non-responsible.
The term 'al-thalatha' refers to these three imams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسكر: [بأن أعتقه وهو سكران، وعند الثلاثة لا يعتق فيهما وقد مر في كتاب الطلاق. (عيني)] أي يصح إذا كان المعتق سكران من شيء محرم، أما إذا كان من مباح كشرب المضطر فهو كالإغماء لا يصح معه تصرف، ولا طلاق ولا عتاق ووجه صحة الإعتاق فيها أن الركن صدر من أهله مضافاً إلى محله كما مر في الطلاق. (مستخلص، فتح)
And [the ruling applies to] a drunk person: [If he emancipates a slave while intoxicated, but according to the three (imams) it is not valid in these two cases, as has passed in the Book of Divorce. ('Ayni)] That is, it is valid if the emancipator is drunk from something forbidden; but if from something permissible, like drinking by a person in dire necessity, it is like unconsciousness: no disposal, nor divorce, nor emancipation is valid with it. The reason for the validity of emancipation in this case is that the pillar came from one qualified, added to its proper place, as has been explained in divorce. (Mustakhlas, Fath)
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This is a ruling about the validity of emancipation (itq) by a person who is intoxicated. The text clarifies that the ruling applies when the drunkenness is from a forbidden (haram) substance. In that case, the emancipation is valid because the essential elements (rukn) are present: the slave is owned, the master is qualified, and the act is directed to its object. However, if the intoxication is from a permissible source, such as drinking out of necessity (which is a hypothetical example; in reality all intoxicants are haram but the distinction is drawn), then the person is like an unconscious one (ighma) and thus no legal disposal (tasarruf), including divorce and emancipation, is valid. The text also notes the disagreement of the three other schools (likely Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) who hold that emancipation by a drunk person is invalid. The Hanafi position is reasoned by the principle that the act is issued by a capable person directed to its object, similar to the case of divorce.

Key Terms
سكران (sukrān): One who is intoxicated; the ruling distinguishes between intoxication from haram (forbidden) and from permissible sources (like medicinal).
الثلاثة (al-thalātha): Refers to the three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) who hold that a drunk person's actions are invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is emancipation (itq) by a drunk person valid?
A) When the drunkenness is from any intoxicant
B) When the drunkenness is from a forbidden substance
C) Only when the person is not known to be drunk
D) Only if the slave consents

Key Takeaway
Emancipation by a person drunk from a forbidden substance is valid in Hanafi law as long as the essential elements are present, but if intoxication is from a permissible source, it is invalid.

Conditional Emancipation: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on conditional emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on conditional emancipation (ta'liq al-itaq) and identify the practical difference between Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on this issue.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, conditional emancipation is valid; a condition already present triggers immediate freedom.

This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling on conditional emancipation (ta'liq al-itaq), where a master suspends a slave's freedom on a future condition. The source passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq, with commentary by al-'Ayni, clarifies that the suspension is valid and the slave becomes free when the condition occurs. It also distinguishes the Hanafi position from that of Imam al-Shafi'i, who does not permit such conditional emancipation when added to future ownership. Understanding this difference is crucial for proper fatwa in matters of emancipation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on ta'liq al-itaq
Identify the core ruling from the matn: that conditional emancipation (ta'liq) is valid when attached to future ownership or a condition. The slave becomes free upon occurrence of the condition.
The matn says: وإن أضافه إلى ملك أو شرط صح
2
Identify the opposing school
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees specifically with the addition to future ownership.
The text says: خلافاً للشافعي في الإضافة إلى الملك
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi school bases its position on the principle that ta'liq is a valid form of expression, analogous to conditional divorce (which is also valid).
The principle of suspension is accepted in other areas of law.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In Hanafi law, if a condition is fulfilled, the slave is free. In Shafi'i law, such an expression is invalid and the slave remains enslaved.
This difference affects actual cases of conditional emancipation.
5
Note the exception for already-existing conditions
If the condition is already present (e.g., saying 'if I own you' while already owning), the Hanafi view treats it as immediate emancipation (tanjiz).
The commentary clarifies this important nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أضافه واعلم أن نسخ المتن اختلفت ففي بعضها هكذا، وفي بعضها ذكر الملك مقيداً بالآتي فهي إن أضاف العتق إلى ملك مستقبل احترز به من نحو إن ملكتك فأنت حر والحال أنه في ملكه الآن فإنه يعتق للحال لما عرف التعليق بالكائن تنجيز. (فتح) إلى ملك بأن قال: إن ملكتك فأنت حر أو إلى سبب الملك كإن اشتريتك فأنت حر. (ط) أو شرط: أي أضافه إلى شرط بأن قال: إن دخلت الدار فأنت حر. (عيني) صح صح التعليق فيعتق عند وجود الشرط خلافاً من للشافعي له في الإضافة إلى الملك. (عيني)
And if he adds it [the emancipation] to ownership or a condition: and know that the copies of the matn differ, so in some it is like this, and in some ownership is mentioned bound by what follows. So if he adds emancipation to future ownership, it guards against cases like saying 'if I own you, you are free' while he currently owns him, then he becomes free immediately because suspension by what is already existing is immediate (tanjiz). (Fath) To ownership: by saying 'if I own you, you are free' or to the cause of ownership like 'if I buy you, you are free'. (T) Or condition: i.e., he adds it to a condition by saying 'if you enter the house, you are free'. ('Ayni) The suspension is valid, so he becomes free upon occurrence of the condition, contrary to al-Shafi'i in addition to ownership. ('Ayni)
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage addresses conditional emancipation (ta'liq al-itaq). The matn states that it is valid when the emancipation is attached to future ownership or to a condition. The commentary by al-'Ayni mentions different versions of the matn: some have 'to ownership or condition' without qualification, while others restrict ownership to future ownership. The effect is that if a master says to his slave whom he already owns, 'if I own you, you are free,' this is treated as immediate emancipation (tanjiz) because the condition is already fulfilled. The phrase 'to ownership' includes expressions like 'if I own you' or 'if I buy you,' and 'to condition' includes 'if you enter the house.' In all these cases, the suspension is valid, and the slave becomes free upon occurrence of the condition. This ruling is in opposition to al-Shafi'i, who does not consider such conditional emancipations valid specifically when added to ownership. The Hanafi school holds that suspension is valid because the act is based on a cause that can occur, similar to conditional divorce.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a legal effect on a future condition.
تنجيز (tanjiz): Immediate execution of an act, without suspension.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a master says to his slave whom he already owns, 'If I own you, you are free,' what is the ruling?
A) The slave becomes free immediately
B) The slave becomes free when the master actually acquires him again
C) The statement is invalid
D) The slave becomes free only if he consents

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, conditional emancipation is valid; a condition already present makes it immediate, while a future condition defers freedom.

Emancipation of a Pregnant Slave and Her Fetus
Ruling statement with rational proofs and distinctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for emancipating a pregnant slave woman and for emancipating only her fetus, and explain why the fetus can be freed but not sold or gifted.

Core Idea
Freeing a pregnant slave frees both; freeing only the fetus frees only the fetus, not the mother.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies two distinct scenarios of emancipation involving a pregnant slave woman. The first scenario is when the master frees the mother herself, and the second is when he frees only the unborn child. The legal principles differ significantly, and the text also contrasts emancipation with sale and gift, highlighting the role of delivery as a condition.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two scenarios
Identify that the passage addresses two separate acts: (1) freeing the pregnant slave woman, and (2) freeing only the fetus. The rulings and consequences for each are different.
The first scenario uses the phrase 'إن حرّر حاملاً' (if he frees a pregnant woman), the second 'وإن حرّره' (and if he frees it, i.e., the fetus).
2
Analyze the first scenario: freeing the mother
When the mother is freed, both she and her child become free automatically, regardless of the master's intention. The child's freedom is either 'asālah' (original) if born within six months (conceived before), or 'tābi'iyyah' (followership) if born after six months. This affects the transfer of wala' (patronage).
Abu Yusuf's exception: if most of the child has emerged at the time of emancipation, it does not become free.
3
Analyze the second scenario: freeing only the fetus
If the master says 'your stomach is free' or similar, only the fetus becomes free. The mother does not become free because freeing her would require including her in the declaration or making her follow the fetus, which would reverse the natural order (the part following the whole).
The fetus is a valid locus for emancipation (mahall li al-‘itq) and is freed both as a follower and when singled out.
4
Compare emancipation with sale and gift
The text asks why emancipation of the fetus is valid while sale and gift are not. The answer is that sale and gift require ability to deliver (qabd) and power of delivery, which is impossible for an unborn fetus. Emancipation has no such condition—it is a pure declaration.
This is a rational proof (dalīl ‘aqlī) distinguishing the contracts.
5
Apply the six-month rule for existence
The fetus is known to have existed at the time of emancipation if the woman gives birth within six months from that date. If she gives birth after six months, the fetus presumably did not exist at the time, so no emancipation occurs.
This rule applies to both scenarios: emancipation takes effect only if the fetus exists when the declaration is made.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاملاً بأن قال لأمة وهي حامل: أنت حرة أو عتقتك. (عيني) عتقا: أي نوى عتقهما أو لم ينو، والحاصل أن الحمل يعتق بعتق أمه مطلقاً سواء ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر أو لأكثر ، فإن وقع العتق عليه أصالة بأن ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر يعتق ولا ينتقل ولائه أبداً إلى موالي أبيه، وإن وقع بمجرد تبعية أمه بأن ولدت لأكثر يعتق أيضاً، لكن إذا أعتق الأب بعده ينجر ولاء ابنه إلى مواليه، وعن أبي يوسف الله: إذا خرج أكثر الولد فأعتق الأم لا يعتق الولد؛ لأنه كالمنفصل في حق الأحكام ألا ترى أنه تنقضي به العدة، ولو مات في هذه الحالة يرث بخلاف ما إذا مات قبل خروج الأكثر رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٣/١] (فتح) وإن حرره عتق فقط أي لو أعتق الحمل خاصة بأن قال : حملك حر أو قال المضغة أو العلقة التي في بطنك حر عتق هو دون الحامل؛ إذ لا وجه لإعتاقها أصالة؛ لأنها لم يضف إليها الإعتاق ولا تبعاً للحمل؛ لأن فيه قلب الموضوع؛ لأن الوضع العقلي أن يكون الحمل تبعاً للأم؛ لأنه كالجزء منها فكون الأم تبعاً للحمل جعل الكل تبعاً للجزء وهو قلب الموضوع فلا تعتق، والحمل محل للعتق، ولهذا يعتق تبعاً للأم فلأن يعتق إذا أفرده أولى. رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٣/١] فإن قيل: لماذا صح العتق في الحمل ولم تصح الهبة والبيع فيه؟ قلنا : القبض وقدرة التسليم شرط في الهبة والبيع ولا قدرة على تسليم الحمل وليس بشرط في العتق فافترقا وإنما يعرف قيام الحمل وقت الإعتاق إذا ولدته لأقل من ستة أشهر ذلك الوقت، وإن ولدته لأكثر من ستة أشهر من ذلك الوقت لم يعتق؛ لعدم التيقن بوجوده في من بطنها وقت الإعتاق. (مستخلص)
Pregnant: By saying to a slave woman while she is pregnant: 'You are free' or 'I have freed you.' ('Ayni) They both become free: i.e., whether he intended both or not. The summary is that the fetus becomes free with the emancipation of its mother absolutely, whether she gives birth before six months or later. If emancipation falls upon it originally by her giving birth before six months, it is free and its patronage never transfers to the father's patrons. If it falls merely by following its mother—by giving birth after six months—it is also free, but if the father frees it afterwards, the patronage of its son is dragged to his patrons. From Abu Yusuf: If most of the child has emerged when the mother is freed, the child does not become free because it is like separated regarding legal rulings. Do you not see that the 'idda is completed by it? And if the child dies in this state, the mother inherits, unlike if it dies before the emergence of most [of its body]. (Rumuz al-Haqaiq, 1/323) (Fath) And if he frees it [i.e., the fetus] alone: meaning if he emancipates only the fetus, by saying 'Your stomach is free' or saying 'the lump of flesh or the clot in your stomach is free,' then it becomes free without the mother. For there is no way to free her originally because she is not included in the emancipation, nor as a follower of the fetus because that would reverse the subject—since rationally the fetus follows the mother as it is part of her, so making the mother follow the fetus would turn the whole into a part, which is a reversal of subject, so she does not become free. The fetus is a locus for emancipation, therefore it becomes free following the mother—so if it is freed alone, it is even more apt. (Rumuz al-Haqaiq, 1/323) Then it is asked: Why is emancipation of the fetus valid while gift and sale of it are not? We say: Delivery and ability to handover are conditions for gift and sale, but there is no ability to hand over the fetus, which is not a condition for emancipation, so they are different. The existence of the fetus at the time of emancipation is known if she gives birth within less than six months from that time; if she gives birth after more than six months, it did not become free due to uncertainty of its existence at that time. (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage deals with the emancipation of a pregnant slave woman and her unborn child. The main ruling is that if the master says to his pregnant slave 'You are free' or 'I have freed you,' both the mother and the child become free, regardless of whether the master intended to free the child or not. This applies whether the child is born after less than six months (indicating it was conceived before the emancipation) or after more than six months (indicating it may have been conceived after). However, there is a nuance: if the child is born within six months of the emancipation, it becomes free as a direct consequence of the mother's freedom, and its wala (patronage) never transfers to the father's patrons. If born after six months, it also becomes free but by way of following the mother, so if the father later frees it, its wala is transferred to the father's patrons because the father's emancipation supersedes. Abu Yusuf holds that if most of the child's body has emerged at the time of the mother's emancipation, the child does not become free because it is considered separate for legal purposes. The text also addresses the scenario where the master frees only the fetus by saying 'Your stomach is free.' In that case, only the fetus becomes free, not the mother, because there is no reason to free her directly or indirectly. A question is then raised: why is emancipating the fetus valid while selling or gifting it is not? The answer is that sale and gift require physical delivery and ability to hand over, which is impossible with a fetus, whereas emancipation does not require delivery—it is a declaration of freedom. Finally, the passage clarifies that the existence of the fetus at the time of emancipation is established if the woman gives birth within six months; if later, the fetus is presumed not to have existed, so it does not become free.

Key Terms
حمل (haml): The fetus in the womb.
ولاء (walā): Patronage relationship established by emancipation, determining inheritance rights and transfer of freed slave's loyalty.
قبض (qabd): Physical delivery or possession; a condition for sale and gift but not for emancipation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what condition makes the emancipation of a fetus effective?
A) The master must intend to free the fetus.
B) The mother must consent to the emancipation.
C) The fetus must exist at the time of emancipation, confirmed by birth within six months.
D) The fetus must be born alive.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rules for emancipating a pregnant slave hinge on whether the mother or only the fetus is freed, with distinct consequences for each and a crucial condition that the fetus must exist at the time of declaration.

The Fetus Follows the Mother in Legal Attributes
Principles of fetal legal status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six attributes in which a fetus follows its mother according to the Hanafi school and explain the legal reasoning (talil) that establishes the mother's preponderance.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a fetus inherits its mother's legal status because the mother's side is preponderant physically and legally.

This lesson unpacks the foundational Hanafi rule that a child in the womb (janin) follows its mother in specific legal attributes. The text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq lists six attributes, and the commentary provides both rational and legal justifications for why the mother's side outweighs the father's in these matters. Understanding this principle is key to many rulings on slavery, freedom, ownership, and inheritance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal rule
Read the opening sentence: 'And the child follows the mother in...' Note that the commentator clarifies 'child' means 'fetus' (haml/janin).
2
List the six attributes
From the text, enumerate: milk, hurriyya, riqq, tadbir, istilad, kitaba. Understand each term in its legal context.
Key terms are defined in the key_terms list.
3
Analyze the talil (reasoning)
The text gives three reasons: (1) the fetus's water is consumed by the mother's, (2) existence is certain from the mother's side (as seen in lineage of fornication and li'an), (3) before birth the fetus is like a limb of the mother in sensation and legal rulings.
This reasoning establishes tarjih (preponderance) of the mother's side.
4
Apply to animals
Note the extension: in animals, the mother determines edibility and eligibility for sacrifice. This shows the principle is general.
5
Contrast with post-birth
After birth, the child does not automatically follow the mother in these attributes: if the mother is freed after delivery, the child is not freed. This is why the commentator preferred 'fetus' over 'child'.
The rule is limited to the prenatal period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والولد يتبع الأمّ في الملك والحرية والرّق والتدبير والاستيلاد والكتابة. وولد الأمة من سيّدها حر. والولد يتبع الأم ولو عبر المصنف بالحمل أو بالجنين بدل الولد لكان أولى؛ لأنه لا يتبع الأم في أوصافها إلا الحمل، وأما الولد بعد الوضع فلا يتبعها في أكثر مما ذكره، حتى لو أعتق الأم بعد الولادة لا يعتق، ووجه تبعية الحمل للأم؛ لأن ماءه يكون مستهلكاً بماءها فترجح جانب الأم؛ ولأنه متيقن به من جانبها؛ ولهذا ثبت نسب ولد الزنا وولد الملاعنة حتى ترثه ويرثها، ولأنه قبل الانفصال كعضو منها حساً وحكماً حتى يتغذى بغذاءها، وينتقل بانتقالها، ويدخل في البيع والعتق وغيرهما من التصرفات تبعاً لها، فكان جانبها أرجح، وكذا يعتبر جانب الأم في البهائم حتى إذا تولد بين الوحشي والأهلي، أو بين المأكول أو غيره، يؤكل إذا كانت أمه تؤكل، ويجوز الأضحية به إذا كانت أمه مما يجوز الأضحية بها، والحاصل أن الحمل يتبع الأم في الأوصاف المذكورة في المتن، ويتبع الأب في النسب؛ لأنه للتعريف، والأم لا تشتهر، ويتبع خير الأبوين في الدين. (عيني، فتح) ماله. (عيني) في الملك: بأن ملكها بشراء أو هبة ونحوهما ملك حملها أيضاً لرجحان جانبها. (عيني) والحرية: أي يتبعها في الحرية بأن حررها وهي حامل يتبعها الحمل كما ذكرنا. (عيني) والرق: أي ويتبع في الرق بأن أسر امرأة من دار الحرب وأخرجها منها بولدها، فولدها رقيق مثلها. (عيني) والتدبير بأن أدبر أمته يتبعها حملها فيه. (عيني) والاستيلاد بأن زوج أم ولده من رجل يكون الولد في حكم أمه، فإذا مات المولى يعتقان من جميع . والكتابة بأن كانت أمة يتبعها حملها في الكتابة. (عيني) وولد الأمة من سيدها حر أي ولد الأمة حال كونه من سيدها حر؛ لأنه مخلوق من مائه فيعتق عليه، لا يعارضه ماء الأمة؛ لأن ماءها مملوك له بخلاف أمة الغير؛ لأن ماءها مملوك لسيدها فتحققت المعارضة فرجحنا جانبها وقلنا: لا يكون حراً ما لم يعتق، والزوج قد رضي بذلك لعلمه به إلا إذا اشترط حرية أولاده، لكن يستثنى منه والد المغرور، أي إذا تزوج امرأة على أنها حرة فإذا هي قنة فولده منها حر بالقيمة؛ لأن الوالد لم يرض به فلهذا لا يتبع للام. رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٤/١] (فتح)
And the child follows the mother in ownership, freedom, slavery, tadbir, istilad, and kitaba. And the child of a slave woman from her master is free. The child follows the mother—if the author had said 'the fetus' or 'the embryo' instead of 'the child' it would have been better, because it is only the fetus that follows the mother in her attributes; as for the child after birth, it does not follow her in most of what he mentioned, so if the mother is freed after delivery, the child is not freed. The reason for the fetus's following the mother is that its water is consumed by her water, so the mother's side outweighs; and because existence is certain from her side, thus the lineage of the child of fornication and the child of li'an is established so that they inherit from each other; and because before separation it is like a limb from her in sensation and ruling, such that it is nourished by her food and moves with her movement, and enters into sale, emancipation and other disposals as a follower of her, so her side is more preponderant. Similarly the mother's side is considered in animals, so that if a birth occurs between a wild and domestic animal, or between an edible and inedible, it is edible if its mother is edible, and it is permissible to sacrifice it if its mother is permissible for sacrifice. The conclusion is that the fetus follows the mother in the attributes mentioned in the text, and follows the father in lineage because it is for identification, and the mother is not well-known, and it follows the better of the two parents in religion. ('Ayni, Fath) His property. ('Ayni) In ownership: if the mother is acquired by purchase or gift etc., the fetus also is owned due to the preponderance of the mother's side. ('Ayni) In freedom: means it follows her in freedom, i.e., if she is freed while pregnant, the fetus follows, as we mentioned. ('Ayni) In slavery: means it follows in slavery, e.g., if a woman from dar al-harb is taken captive and brought out with her child, the child is a slave like her. ('Ayni) In tadbir: if a master declares his slave woman mudabbara, the pregnancy follows her. ('Ayni) In istilad: if the master marries his umm walad to a man, the child is in the same ruling as the mother, so upon the master's death, both are freed from all [the estate]. In kitaba: if the slave is under kitaba, the fetus follows her. ('Ayni) And the child of a slave woman from her master: means the child of a slave woman when it is from her master is free, because it is created from his semen, so it becomes free upon him. The water of the slave woman does not oppose it because her water is owned by him; unlike a slave woman belonging to another, because her water is owned by her master, so there is opposition, we preponderate her side and say: it is not free until emancipated. The husband has consented to this with his knowledge, unless he stipulated the freedom of his children. However, the case of the deceived husband (al-maghrur) is excluded: if a man marries a woman thinking she is free, but she is a concubine, then his child from her is free with payment of its value because the father did not consent to this, so it does not follow the mother. (Rumuz al-Haqaiq, 1/324) (Fath)
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage establishes the principle that the child in the womb (fetus) follows its mother in specific legal attributes: ownership (milk), freedom (hurriyya), slavery (riqq), tadbir (declaration that the slave will be free upon the master's death), istilad (being a child's mother who is free upon the master's death), and kitaba (contract of manumission by payment). After birth, the child does not necessarily follow the mother; for example, if the mother is freed after birth, the child does not become free. The commentary provides reasons: the mother's side is more preponderant because the fetus is formed from her and is considered part of her body and rulings. The principle also applies to animals for edible and sacrifice rulings. After enumerating the attributes, the text also states the rule that the child of a slave woman from her master is free, because it is created from the master's semen, which is not owned by the slave woman. However, this is only if the child is from the master's own slave woman. If it is from someone else's slave woman, the child is not free unless emancipated. An exception is the case of a deceived husband (al-maghrur) who marries a woman thinking she is free but she is a slave; his child is free with a monetary compensation because he did not consent to the enslavement of his child. This shows the strength of the father's side in such a case.

Key Terms
الملك (al-milk): Ownership of the mother; the fetus is included in her ownership.
الحرية (al-hurriyya): Freedom; if the mother is freed while pregnant, the fetus becomes free.
الرق (al-riqq): Slavery; if the mother is enslaved, the fetus is also enslaved.
التدبير (al-tadbir): Declaring a slave free after the master's death; the fetus follows the mother in this.
الاستيلاد (al-istilad): Status of a slave woman who bears a child to her master; the child follows the mother's status.
الكتابة (al-kitaba): Contract of manumission by payment; the fetus follows the mother in kitaba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a pregnant slave woman is purchased by a new owner, the fetus:
A) Remains free because it is a human
B) Becomes the property of the new owner along with the mother
C) Is not included in the sale
D) Becomes free if the father was free

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a fetus shares its mother's legal status in six specific attributes due to the mother's preponderant side.

Exceptions: The Child of the Master and the Deceived Husband
Exceptions to the fetal following rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi rulings on the child of a master from his own slave woman and the child of a deceived husband (al-maghrur), and apply the qiyas reasoning to determine the child's status.

Core Idea
The child of a master from his own slave woman is free; but if the mother belongs to another or the father was deceived, different rules apply based on ownership and consent.

This lesson delves into two important exceptions appended to the general principle. First, the rule that a child born to a master from his own slave woman is free (walad al-ammah min sayyidiha hurr). Second, the exceptional case of al-maghrur (the deceived husband) where the child is free with compensation. Understanding the underlying qiyas (analogy) is crucial for grasping when the father's side prevails over the mother's.

Guided Path
1
State the rule for child of master
Read: 'And the child of a slave woman from her master is free.' Understand that this applies when the master impregnates his own slave woman.
2
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
The text says: 'because it is created from his semen, so it becomes free upon him. The water of the slave woman does not oppose it because her water is owned by him.'
The master owns both elements, so the child is free.
3
Contrast with child of another's slave woman
If the woman belongs to another, the mother's water is owned by her master, creating opposition. The mother's side is preponderated, so the child is not free unless emancipated.
This contrast is a form of qiyas.
4
Examine the exception: al-maghrur
A man marries a woman thinking she is free, but she is a slave. His child from her is free with payment of its value, because he did not consent to the child being a slave.
The father's intent and consent override the mother's status.
5
Determine the common principle
The child's status is determined by whether the father has ownership over the mother or has consented to her status; if not, the child follows the mother's slavery unless the father is deceived.
Consent and ownership are key factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني، فتح) ماله. (عيني) في الملك: بأن ملكها بشراء أو هبة ونحوهما ملك حملها أيضاً لرجحان جانبها. (عيني) والحرية: أي يتبعها في الحرية بأن حررها وهي حامل يتبعها الحمل كما ذكرنا. (عيني) والرق: أي ويتبع في الرق بأن أسر امرأة من دار الحرب وأخرجها منها بولدها، فولدها رقيق مثلها. (عيني) والتدبير بأن أدبر أمته يتبعها حملها فيه. (عيني) والاستيلاد بأن زوج أم ولده من رجل يكون الولد في حكم أمه، فإذا مات المولى يعتقان من جميع . والكتابة بأن كانت أمة يتبعها حملها في الكتابة. (عيني) وولد الأمة من سيدها حر أي ولد الأمة حال كونه من سيدها حر؛ لأنه مخلوق من مائه فيعتق عليه، لا يعارضه ماء الأمة؛ لأن ماءها مملوك له بخلاف أمة الغير؛ لأن ماءها مملوك لسيدها فتحققت المعارضة فرجحنا جانبها وقلنا: لا يكون حراً ما لم يعتق، والزوج قد رضي بذلك لعلمه به إلا إذا اشترط حرية أولاده، لكن يستثنى منه والد المغرور، أي إذا تزوج امرأة على أنها حرة فإذا هي قنة فولده منها حر بالقيمة؛ لأن الوالد لم يرض به فلهذا لا يتبع للام. رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٤/١] (فتح)

('Ayni) In istilad: if the master marries his umm walad to a man, the child is in the same ruling as the mother, so upon the master's death, both are freed from all [the estate]. In kitaba: if the slave is under kitaba, the fetus follows her. ('Ayni) And the child of a slave woman from her master: means the child of a slave woman when it is from her master is free, because it is created from his semen, so it becomes free upon him. The water of the slave woman does not oppose it because her water is owned by him; unlike a slave woman belonging to another, because her water is owned by her master, so there is opposition, we preponderate her side and say: it is not free until emancipated. The husband has consented to this with his knowledge, unless he stipulated the freedom of his children. However, the case of the deceived husband (al-maghrur) is excluded: if a man marries a woman thinking she is free, but she is a concubine, then his child from her is free with payment of its value because the father did not consent to this, so it does not follow the mother. (Rumuz al-Haqaiq, 1/324) (Fath)
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage establishes the principle that the child in the womb (fetus) follows its mother in specific legal attributes: ownership (milk), freedom (hurriyya), slavery (riqq), tadbir (declaration that the slave will be free upon the master's death), istilad (being a child's mother who is free upon the master's death), and kitaba (contract of manumission by payment). After birth, the child does not necessarily follow the mother; for example, if the mother is freed after birth, the child does not become free. The commentary provides reasons: the mother's side is more preponderant because the fetus is formed from her and is considered part of her body and rulings. The principle also applies to animals for edible and sacrifice rulings. After enumerating the attributes, the text also states the rule that the child of a slave woman from her master is free, because it is created from the master's semen, which is not owned by the slave woman. However, this is only if the child is from the master's own slave woman. If it is from someone else's slave woman, the child is not free unless emancipated. An exception is the case of a deceived husband (al-maghrur) who marries a woman thinking she is free but she is a slave; his child is free with a monetary compensation because he did not consent to the enslavement of his child. This shows the strength of the father's side in such a case.

Key Terms
المغرور (al-maghrūr): A deceived husband who marries a woman thinking she is free, but she is a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a free man marries a woman believing she is free, but she is actually a slave, the child from this marriage is:
A) A slave, because the fetus follows the mother
B) Free, without any compensation
C) Free, but the father must pay the child's value to the owner
D) A slave unless the father stipulates freedom

Key Takeaway
The child of a master from his slave woman is free; if the father was deceived about the mother's slavery, the child is free with compensation.

The Fetus Follows the Mother: A Foundational Hanafi Rule
Ruling and rational proof that the fetus follows the mother in six legal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six attributes in which the fetus follows the mother, explain the rational proof, and identify the exception for al-maghruur.

Core Idea
The fetus follows the mother in six legal attributes because it is legally part of her before birth.

This passage from Kitab al-Itaq establishes a key Hanafi rule: the unborn child (haml) follows the mother in six specific attributes—ownership, freedom, slavery, tadbir, istilad, and kitabah. The commentator al-Ayni clarifies that the rule applies only to the fetus, not the child after birth, and provides a rational proof based on the fetus being absorbed into the mother's fluid and treated as her limb. We will walk through the rule, its proof, and its applications, including the exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six attributes and the general rule
Read the opening statement and list the six attributes: al-milk (ownership), al-hurriyya (freedom), al-riqq (slavery), al-tadbir (tadbir), al-istilad (istilad), and al-kitaba (kitabah). Note that the author uses 'al-walad' but the commentator refines it.
The rule is a qa'ida fiqhiyya, not a specific hukm for an act.
2
Understand the commentator's refinement: al-walad vs al-haml
Al-Ayni states that using 'al-haml' (fetus) would be more precise because after birth, the child does not follow the mother in most of these attributes. For example, if a mother is freed after delivery, the child is not freed.
This shows the importance of precise terminology (tahqiq al-manat).
3
Analyze the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The proof has three parts: (1) the fetus's fluid is absorbed by the mother's, so her side preponderates; (2) the fetus is certain from her side (as seen in lineage rules for fornication and li'an); (3) before separation, the fetus is like a limb of the mother physically and legally—it shares nourishment, movement, and is included in transactions concerning her.
This proof is based on istihsan and the physical unity of mother and fetus.
4
Examine how each attribute applies in practice
Go through each attribute: ownership (purchase of mother includes fetus), freedom (freeing mother frees fetus), slavery (capture of mother enslaves fetus), tadbir (making mother mudabbara makes fetus mudabbar), istilad (if mother is umm walad and married, fetus follows her status), kitabah (mother's kitabah includes fetus).
Each application reinforces the general rule.
5
Note the exception for the child of the master and the case of al-maghruur
The child of a female slave from her master is free because it is created from his property (his fluid). But if the slave belongs to another, the child is not free unless freed. An exception to this exception is the case of al-maghruur (deceived husband): if a man marries thinking she is free, his child is free with compensation.
This exception protects the father's consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب العتاق
أحكام العتاق والولد يتبع الأمّ في الملك والحرية والرّق والتدبير والاستيلاد والكتابة. وولد الأمة من سيّدها حر.
والولد يتبع الأم ولو عبر المصنف بالحمل أو بالجنين بدل الولد لكان أولى؛ لأنه لا يتبع الأم في أوصافها إلا الحمل، وأما الولد بعد الوضع فلا يتبعها في أكثر مما ذكره، حتى لو أعتق الأم بعد الولادة لا يعتق، ووجه تبعية الحمل للأم؛ لأن ماءه يكون مستهلكاً بماءها فترجح جانب الأم؛ ولأنه متيقن به من جانبها؛ ولهذا ثبت نسب ولد الزنا وولد الملاعنة حتى ترثه ويرثها، ولأنه قبل الانفصال كعضو منها حساً وحكماً حتى يتغذى بغذاءها، وينتقل بانتقالها، ويدخل في البيع والعتق وغيرهما من التصرفات تبعاً لها، فكان جانبها أرجح، وكذا يعتبر جانب الأم في البهائم حتى إذا تولد بين الوحشي والأهلي، أو بين المأكول أو غيره، يؤكل إذا كانت أمه تؤكل، ويجوز الأضحية به إذا كانت أمه مما يجوز الأضحية بها، والحاصل أن الحمل يتبع الأم في الأوصاف المذكورة في المتن، ويتبع الأب في النسب؛ لأنه للتعريف، والأم لا تشتهر، ويتبع خير الأبوين في الدين. (عيني، فتح)
ماله. (عيني)

(عيني) والرق: أي ويتبع في الرق بأن أسر امرأة من دار الحرب وأخرجها منها بولدها، فولدها رقيق مثلها. (عيني) والتدبير بأن أدبر أمته يتبعها حملها فيه. (عيني) والاستيلاد بأن زوج أم ولده من رجل يكون الولد في حكم أمه، فإذا مات المولى يعتقان من جميع . والكتابة بأن كانت أمة يتبعها حملها في الكتابة. (عيني) وولد الأمة من سيدها حر أي ولد الأمة حال كونه من سيدها حر؛ لأنه مخلوق من مائه فيعتق عليه، لا يعارضه ماء الأمة؛ لأن ماءها مملوك له بخلاف أمة الغير؛ لأن ماءها مملوك لسيدها فتحققت المعارضة فرجحنا جانبها وقلنا: لا يكون حراً ما لم يعتق، والزوج قد رضي بذلك لعلمه به إلا إذا اشترط حرية أولاده، لكن يستثنى منه والد المغرور، أي إذا تزوج امرأة على أنها حرة فإذا هي قنة فولده منها حر بالقيمة؛ لأن الوالد لم يرض به فلهذا لا يتبع للام. رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٤/١] (فتح)
Book of Emancipation. Rulings of emancipation: The child follows the mother in ownership, freedom, slavery, tadbir, istilad, and kitabah. And the child of the female slave from her master is free. The child follows the mother. If the author had used 'the fetus' or 'the embryo' instead of 'the child', it would have been better; because the child does not follow the mother in her attributes except the fetus. As for the child after birth, it does not follow her in most of what he mentioned, such that if he freed the mother after childbirth, the child does not become free. And the rationale for the fetus following the mother: because his fluid is absorbed by her fluid, so the mother's side preponderates; and because it is certain from her side; therefore the lineage of the child of fornication and the child of li'an is established, so that she inherits from him and he from her; and because before separation, it is like a limb of her physically and legally, such that it is nourished by her food, moves with her movement, and enters into sale, emancipation, and other actions as a dependent on her, so her side is stronger. Similarly, the mother's side is considered in animals, such that if there is a cross between a wild and domesticated animal, or between edible and otherwise, it is eaten if its mother is edible, and it is permissible for sacrifice if its mother is of the type permissible for sacrifice. The conclusion is that the fetus follows the mother in the attributes mentioned in the text, and follows the father in lineage because it is for identification, and the mother is not known, and follows the better of the two parents in religion. (Ayni, Fath) His property. (Ayni) Regarding ownership: If he owns her by purchase or gift or similar, he also owns her fetus due to the preponderance of her side. (Ayni) Freedom: meaning the child follows her in freedom such that if he frees her while she is pregnant, the fetus follows her in freedom as we mentioned. (Ayni) Slavery: meaning the child follows her in slavery such that if he captures a woman from dar al-harb and brings her out with her child, her child is a slave like her. (Ayni) Tadbir: If he makes his female slave a mudabbara, her fetus follows her in that. (Ayni) Istilad: If he marries his umm walad to a man, the child is in the status of his mother, so when the master dies, they both become free from the entire estate. Kitabah: If a female slave is given kitabah, her fetus follows her in kitabah. (Ayni) And the child of the female slave from her master is free: i.e., the child of the female slave while being from her master is free because he is created from his fluid, so he becomes free by him; the fluid of the female slave does not oppose it because her fluid is owned by the master, unlike the female slave of another because her fluid is owned by her master, so opposition is realized, and we preferred her side and said: he is not free unless he is emancipated. And the husband is content with that because he knows it, unless he stipulated the freedom of his children. But excepted from this is the father of the deceived (al-maghruur), i.e., if a man marries a woman thinking she is free, and she turns out to be a female slave, his child from her is free with compensation because the father did not consent to this, so for that reason the child does not follow the mother. (Rumuz al-Haqaiq: 1/324) (Fath)
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the opening of Kitab al-Itaq. The author states the general principle: the child follows the mother in six specific attributes: ownership, freedom, slavery, tadbir, istilad, and kitabah. He then adds the exception that the child of a female slave from her master is free. The commentator (Ayni) refines this by noting that the author should have used 'al-haml' (the fetus) instead of 'al-walad' (the child), because after birth the child does not automatically follow the mother in all these attributes; only the fetus does. For example, if the mother is freed after delivery, the child is not freed. The proof for the fetus following the mother is rational: the father's fluid is absorbed by the mother's, making her side dominant; the fetus is certain from her side (proven by rules of lineage in fornication and li'an); and before birth it is physically and legally part of her—it shares her nourishment, movements, and is included in transactions concerning her. The same principle applies to animals: the young animal follows the mother in being edible or suitable for sacrifice. The passage also explains each attribute individually: in ownership, purchase of a pregnant slave includes her fetus; in freedom, freeing a pregnant slave frees the fetus; in slavery, capture of a pregnant woman enslaves her fetus; and similarly for tadbir, istilad, and kitabah. The rule that the child of a master from his female slave is free is justified because the child is created from the master's fluid, which is his own property, so there is no opposition; but if the slave belongs to another, the child is not free unless freed. An exception is the case of al-maghruur (the deceived husband): if a man marries a woman thinking she is free and she turns out to be a slave, the child is free with compensation, because the father did not consent.

Key Terms
الولد (al-walad): The child after birth; the author uses it generally but the commentator notes that it is more precise to say al-haml (the fetus) because after birth the child does not follow the mother in all attributes.
الحمل (al-haml): The fetus or unborn child, which is treated as following the mother in the six attributes until birth.
المغرور (al-maghruur): The deceived husband who marries a woman thinking she is free but she is a slave; his child from her is free with compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the term 'al-walad' is grammatically incorrect.
B) Because the child after birth does not follow the mother in the mentioned attributes, only the fetus does.
C) Because the fetus is not considered a child until it is born.
D) Because the author forgot to mention the exceptions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule that the fetus follows the mother in six attributes is based on the legal fiction that the fetus is part of the mother; after birth, the child becomes independent.

Partial Emancipation: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether freeing part of a slave frees the whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on partial emancipation, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the ruling to joint ownership scenarios.

Core Idea
Freeing part of a slave does not free the whole: the slave must work to pay the remaining value.

The moment a master says 'You are half free', a deep legal question arises: does that declaration make the entire slave free, or only part? The Hanafi school's answer depends on how we define emancipation itself — as removal of ownership (divisible) or removal of slavery (indivisible). This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq unpacks the intra-madhhab split between Abu Hanifa and his two great students, with far-reaching practical consequences for the slave's status.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify the ruling as presented in the matn: if someone frees part of his slave, the whole is not freed; the slave must work to pay the remaining value, like a mukatab.
The matn makes this the primary ruling.
2
Identify the opposing view (Sahibayn)
Recognise that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that freeing part frees the whole slave immediately, because emancipation is indivisible.
Imam al-Shafi'i and other schools agree with the Sahibayn.
3
Analyse the conceptual dispute
Examine the four concepts: slavery (indivisible), freedom (indivisible), removal of ownership (divisible by consensus), and the act of emancipation. Abu Hanifa sees emancipation as removal of ownership (divisible); the Sahibayn see it as removal of slavery or establishment of freedom (indivisible).
This difference in the nature of emancipation drives the entire dispute.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
List the practical consequences: under Abu Hanifa, the slave cannot testify, inherit, or marry until he completes payment; under the Sahibayn, he is fully free with a debt, so he has full legal capacity.
Also, if the slave is jointly owned, the co-owner's options differ.
5
Note the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa, as affirmed by the matn and later commentators.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العبد الذي يعتق بعضه
من أعتق بعض عبده لم يعتق كله وسعى له فيما بقى، وهو كالمكاتب، وإن أعتق أي لسيده من قيمته أي معتق البعض نصيبه فلشريكه أن يحرّر أو يستشعي، والولاء لهما، أو يضمن لو موسراً، ويرجع من عبد مشترك الخيار بين ثلاثة أشياء به على العبد، أي بالذي ضمن وأدى العبد في قيمة نصيبه للشريكين في الوجهين ما دام يسعى المعتق
باب العبد: أي في بيان أحكامه هل يقتصر عليه أو يعتق كله رمز الحقائق ٣٢٤/١٥] بعض عبده: بأن أعتق ربعه أو ثلثه أو نصفه رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٤/١]
لم يعتق كله ولا بعضه بل يزول الملك عن البعض، ويتأخر العتق إلى زوال الملك عن الكل بالسعاية عند أبي حنيفة، ولهذا كان رقيقاً في شهادته وسائر أحكامه وههنا أشياء أربعة : الرق والعتق وإزالة الملك والإعتاق، وأحكامها أربعة وهو أن الرق والعتق لا يتجزءان وإزالة الملك متجزئ إجماعاً، والإعتاق إزالة الملك عنده، وإثبات العتق عندهما، وأصله أن الإعتاق متجزئ عنده فيقتصر على ما أعتق؛ إذ الإعتاق إزالة الملك، وعندهما لا يتجزأ بمعنى أن إعتاق البعض إعتاق للكل؛ لأنه إما إثبات العتق أو إسقاط الرق، وهما لا يتجزءان، فلا يتجزأ الإعتاق ضرورة، فالإعتاق يوجب زوال الملك عنده وهو متجزئ، وعندهما يوجب زوال الرق وهو غير متجزئ. وأما نفس الإعتاق أو العتق فلا يتجزأ بالإجماع؛ لأن ذات القول وهو العلة وحكمه وهو نزول الحرية فيه لا يتصور فيه التجزي، وكذا الرق لا يتجزأ بالإجماع؛ لأنه ضعف حكمي، والحرية قوة حكمية، فلا يتصور اجتماعهما في شخص واحد، فأبو حنيفة اعتبر جانب الرق فجعل كله رقيقاً على ما كان وقال: زال ملكه عن البعض الذي أعتقه ولم يكن ذلك البعض حراً، وهما اعتبرا جانب الحرية فصار كله حراً، وبه قالت الثلاثة، ولو قال: بعضك حر أو جزء منك حر يؤمر بالبيان، ولو قال: سهم منك حر، عتق سدسه كما في الوصية بالسهم من عبده، وعندهما: يعتق كله في الكل. (عيني، فتح)
فيما بقي من قيمته، فإذا أدى عتق كله دفعة واحدة. (عيني) وهو كالمكاتب عند أبي حنيفة له؛ لأنه كله رقيق وإنما زال الملك عن بعضه بإضافة العتق إليه فعمل بالدليلين بإنزاله مكاتباً حتى لا تقبل شهادته ولا يرث ولا يورث ولا يتزوج إلا أنه إذا عجز لا يرد إلى الرق؛ لأن المولى أوجب السعاية لوقوع الحرية في البعض، وهو موجود بعد العجز، بخلاف المكاتب كتابة مقصودة، وعندهما هو كالحر المديون؛ لأن العتق وقع في جميعه بناء على ما تقدم، وهو قول الشافعي له، ولا سعاية عليه رمز الحقائق: ٣٢٥/١] (مستخلص) أن يحرر : إما أن يعتق نصيبه. (عيني) أو يضمن أي المعتق قيمة نصيبه للشريك (عيني) لو موسراً: أي لو كان المعتق غنياً بأن يكون مالكاً قدر قيمة نصيب الآخر. (ط)
Chapter on the slave who is partially emancipated. Whoever frees part of his slave does not free all of him, and he [the slave] must work for the remaining part, and he is like a mukatab. And if he frees [a part], i.e., for his master, from his value, i.e., the one who freed part of his share [in a jointly owned slave], then his partner has the option to either free [his share] or demand work [from the slave], and the wala' belongs to both, or to guarantee [the value] if [the freeing slave] is wealthy, and [the partner] has recourse to the slave for what he guaranteed. [The slave] pays the value of the partner's share in both cases as long as the freed one works. (Commentary:) The chapter on the slave: i.e., explaining his rulings: whether it remains on him or becomes all free. (Rumuz al-Haqaiq: 15/324) 'Part of his slave': i.e., he freed a quarter, a third, or a half (Rumuz: 1/324). According to Abu Hanifa: he is not freed entirely nor partially, but ownership of the part is removed, and freedom is delayed until ownership of the whole is removed by working. Therefore he remains a slave in terms of testimony and all other rulings. And here there are four things: slavery, freedom, removal of ownership, and emancipation. Their rulings are four: that slavery and freedom are indivisible, while removal of ownership is divisible by consensus. Emancipation is removal of ownership according to him (Abu Hanifa), and establishment of freedom according to them (the two companions). The origin is that emancipation is divisible according to him, so it is confined to what he freed, because emancipation is removal of ownership. According to them, it is indivisible; meaning that freeing part is freeing the whole, because it is either establishing freedom or removing slavery, both of which are indivisible, so emancipation is necessarily indivisible. So emancipation causes removal of ownership according to him, which is divisible; according to them, it causes removal of slavery, which is indivisible. As for the act of emancipation or freedom itself, it is indivisible by consensus, because the words themselves (the cause) and their effect (the descent of freedom) cannot be divided. Similarly, slavery is indivisible by consensus, because it is a legal weakness, and freedom is legal strength; they cannot coexist in one person. So Abu Hanifa considered the aspect of slavery and made him entirely a slave as before, and said: his ownership of the freed part is removed, but that part is not free. The two considered the aspect of freedom, so the whole becomes free, and the three [other imams] said this. If he says: 'Some part of you is free' or 'A portion of you is free', he is ordered to clarify. If he says: 'A share of you is free', then one-sixth of him becomes free, as in the case of bequeathing a share of his slave; according to them, the whole becomes free in all cases. (Ayni, Fath) He works for the remainder of his value; when he pays it, he becomes completely free at once. (Ayni) According to Abu Hanifa, he is like a mukatab, because he is entirely a slave, but ownership of part of him has been removed by attaching the emancipation to it; thus he acted upon both proofs by making him a mukatab, so his testimony is not accepted, he does not inherit, is not inherited, and does not marry. However, if he becomes unable, he is not returned to slavery, because the master obligated the working due to the occurrence of freedom in part, which remains even after inability, unlike a mukatab with intended kitabah. According to them, he is like a free debtor because emancipation occurred on his whole based on what preceded, and it is the view of al-Shafi'i; and no working is required. (Rumuz: 325/1) (Mukhtasar). He [the partner] may free: i.e., he frees his share. (Ayni) Or guarantee: i.e., the freeing slave guarantees the value of the partner's share (Ayni) if he is wealthy: i.e., if the freeing slave is rich, owning the equivalent of the other's share. (T)
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding partial emancipation of a slave. The matn states that if someone frees part of his slave (e.g., a quarter or half), the whole does not become free, and the slave must work to pay the remaining value, like a mukatab. If the slave is jointly owned and one partner frees his share, the other partner has three options: (1) free his own share, (2) demand that the slave work for his share's value, or (3) demand that the freeing partner guarantee the value if he is wealthy. The commentator explains the dispute's theoretical basis: the key concepts are slavery, freedom, removal of ownership, and emancipation. By consensus, slavery and freedom are indivisible (a person cannot be partly slave and partly free). Removal of ownership is divisible. The dispute is over the nature of emancipation. Abu Hanifa considers emancipation as removal of ownership, which is divisible, so only the freed part's ownership is removed, but the slave remains a slave; freedom is postponed until the whole value is paid. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) consider emancipation as removal of slavery (or establishment of freedom), which is indivisible, so freeing part frees the whole. Imam al-Shafi'i agrees with them. The practical consequences differ: according to Abu Hanifa, the slave is like a mukatab (his testimony is not accepted, he does not inherit, etc.), but if he becomes unable to pay, he is not returned to slavery because the master's declaration has already effectuated some freedom. According to the companions, he is like a free man with debt, so his testimony is accepted, he can marry, etc., and he does not have to work for the remaining value—the partner's share is treated as a debt. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa, as the text indicates with the initial ruling and the detailed explanation.

Key Terms
السعاية (al-sa'ayah): The work required of a partially freed slave to pay the remaining value of himself; like kitabah but not exactly.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master to pay a sum for his freedom. The partially freed slave is compared to a mukatab but with differences.
العتق (al-'itq): Emancipation; the act of freeing a slave. The dispute centers on whether it is divisible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the legal status of a slave who is partially emancipated (e.g., his master frees half of him)?
A) The slave becomes completely free immediately.
B) The slave is entirely free except he must pay half his value.
C) The slave remains a slave in all rulings but must work to pay the remaining value, similar to a mukatab.
D) The slave becomes free only in the part that was freed, and the rest remains enslaved.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, partially freeing a slave does not grant immediate freedom; the slave must work to pay the remaining value, reflecting the view that emancipation is divisible as removal of ownership.

Partial Emancipation and the Divisibility of 'Itq
Explaining the ruling and the intra-Hanafi dispute on partial emancipation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on partial emancipation and explain how the definition of 'itq (removal of ownership vs. affirmation of freedom) determines the ruling.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the dispute over partial emancipation hinges on whether 'itq is divisible removal of ownership or indivisible affirmation of freedom.

This passage explores a classic intra-Hanafi disagreement: if a master frees half his slave, does the slave become fully free? The answer depends on how we define emancipation itself—as removal of ownership (divisible) or as bestowal of freedom (indivisible). The ruling of Abu Hanifa and his two companions diverge on this conceptual point, with practical consequences for the slave's legal status.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Abu Hanifa holds that emancipating part of a slave only removes the master's ownership of that part. The slave does not become free, even partially, because slavery and freedom cannot coexist. Instead, the slave must work (sa'y) to pay the value of the remaining part, similar to a mukatab.
The slave remains a slave in all legal rulings (e.g., testimony) until the full value is paid.
2
Identify the opponent (the two companions)
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani argue that emancipating part of a slave frees the entire slave immediately. They consider 'itq to be the affirmation of freedom (ithbat al-'itq), which is indivisible.
Their view aligns with the majority of other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali).
3
Understand the four concepts and their divisibility
The passage identifies four concepts: slavery (raqq), freedom (itq), removal of ownership (izalat al-milk), and emancipation (i'taq). Slavery and freedom are indivisible by consensus. Removal of ownership is divisible by consensus. The disagreement is over the nature of emancipation: is it removal of ownership (divisible) or affirmation of freedom (indivisible)?
This conceptual framework is the key to the dispute.
4
Analyze the proof: talil of the disagreement
Abu Hanifa defines 'itq as removal of ownership (izalat al-milk), which is divisible. Therefore, emancipating part only removes ownership of that part, leaving the slave still a slave overall. The two companions define 'itq as affirmation of freedom (ithbat al-'itq), which is indivisible, so freeing part frees the whole.
Each position logically follows from its definition of 'itq.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the slave remains a slave until full payment, so he cannot testify, and his inheritance and other rulings remain those of a slave. Under the companions' view, the slave becomes immediately free, with full legal capacity. The fatwa relies on the companions' view as more favorable to freedom.
The practical difference is significant for the slave's legal status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العبد الذي يعتق بعضه من أعتق بعض عبده لم يعتق كله وسعى له فيما بقى، وهو كالمكاتب، وإن أعتق أي لسيده من قيمته أي معتق البعض نصيبه فلشريكه أن يحرّر أو يستشعي، والولاء لهما، أو يضمن لو موسراً، ويرجع به على العبد، أي بالذي ضمن وأدى العبد في قيمة نصيبه للشريكين في الوجهين ما دام يسعى المعتق. لم يعتق كله ولا بعضه بل يزول الملك عن البعض، ويتأخر العتق إلى زوال الملك عن الكل بالسعاية عند أبي حنيفة، ولهذا كان رقيقاً في شهادته وسائر أحكامه وههنا أشياء أربعة : الرق والعتق وإزالة الملك والإعتاق، وأحكامها أربعة وهو أن الرق والعتق لا يتجزءان وإزالة الملك متجزئ إجماعاً، والإعتاق إزالة الملك عنده، وإثبات العتق عندهما، وأصله أن الإعتاق متجزئ عنده فيقتصر على ما أعتق؛ إذ الإعتاق إزالة الملك، وعندهما لا يتجزأ بمعنى أن إعتاق البعض إعتاق للكل؛ لأنه إما إثبات العتق أو إسقاط الرق، وهما لا يتجزءان، فلا يتجزأ الإعتاق ضرورة، فالإعتاق يوجب زوال الملك عنده وهو متجزئ، وعندهما يوجب زوال الرق وهو غير متجزئ. وأما نفس الإعتاق أو العتق فلا يتجزأ بالإجماع؛ لأن ذات القول وهو العلة وحكمه وهو نزول الحرية فيه لا يتصور فيه التجزي، وكذا الرق لا يتجزأ بالإجماع؛ لأنه ضعف حكمي، والحرية قوة حكمية، فلا يتصور اجتماعهما في شخص واحد، فأبو حنيفة اعتبر جانب الرق فجعل كله رقيقاً على ما كان وقال: زال ملكه عن البعض الذي أعتقه ولم يكن ذلك البعض حراً، وهما اعتبرا جانب الحرية فصار كله حراً، وبه قالت الثلاثة
Chapter on the slave who is partially emancipated. Whoever emancipates part of his slave, it is not fully emancipated, and he (the slave) must work (sa'y) for what remains, and he is like a mukatab. And if he emancipates—meaning for his master from his value, i.e., the one who emancipates part of his share—then his partner may either free (his own share) or seek service (sa'y), and the wala' belongs to both, or he (the partner) may guarantee (daman) if (the emancipator) is wealthy, and he (the guarantor) is reimbursed by the slave for the value of his share for both partners in both cases as long as the emancipated slave works. He is not fully free nor partially free, rather ownership is removed from the part, and emancipation is delayed until ownership is completely removed through sa'y according to Abu Hanifa. Therefore, he remains a slave in his testimony and all other rulings. Here there are four things: slavery, freedom, removal of ownership, and emancipation. Their rulings are four: that slavery and freedom are indivisible, removal of ownership is divisible by consensus, and emancipation is removal of ownership according to him (Abu Hanifa), and affirmation of freedom according to them (the two companions). The principle is that emancipation is divisible according to him, so it is confined to what he emancipated, because emancipation is removal of ownership. According to them, it is indivisible in the sense that emancipating a part is emancipating the whole, because it is either affirmation of freedom or removal of slavery, and both are indivisible, so emancipation is necessarily indivisible. Thus emancipation necessitates removal of ownership according to him, which is divisible, and according to them, it necessitates removal of slavery, which is indivisible. As for the act of emancipation or freedom itself, it is indivisible by consensus, because the essence of the statement, which is the cause, and its ruling, which is the descent of freedom, cannot be imagined to be divided. Similarly, slavery is indivisible by consensus, because it is a legal weakness, and freedom is a legal strength, so their coexistence in one person is inconceivable. So Abu Hanifa considered the aspect of slavery and made the whole a slave as before, and said: his ownership of the part he emancipated is removed, but that part is not free. The two companions considered the aspect of freedom, so the whole becomes free, and the three (imams) also said this.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the slave who is partially emancipated. The author first states the ruling: if someone emancipates part of his slave, the slave does not become fully free nor even partially free. Instead, the slave must work (sa'y) to pay the value of the remaining part, similar to a mukatab. The partner of the emancipator has options: he can free his own share, or demand service from the slave, or if the emancipator is wealthy, he can demand a guarantee (daman) from the emancipator for the value of his share, and then the emancipator can reclaim that amount from the slave. The passage then delves into the fundamental disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the nature of emancipation ('itq). Abu Hanifa holds that 'itq is divisible because it is essentially the removal of ownership (izalat al-milk), which is divisible. Therefore, only the part that was emancipated has its ownership removed, but the slave is still considered a slave overall, as slavery and freedom cannot coexist. Thus, the slave remains a slave in all legal rulings (e.g., testimony, inheritance) until the full value is paid. The two companions, supported by the three other imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad), argue that 'itq is the affirmation of freedom (ithbat al-'itq) or the removal of slavery (isqat al-raqq), both of which are indivisible. Hence, emancipating part results in the entire slave becoming free. This is a classic intra-Hanafi dispute rooted in the definition of 'itq.

Key Terms
إعتاق (i'taq): The act of emancipating a slave; in this passage, it is analyzed as either removal of ownership (izalat al-milk) or affirmation of freedom (ithbat al-'itq).
سعاية (sa'y): The slave's earning of money through work to pay for the remaining value of his person.
إزالة الملك (izalat al-milk): Removal of ownership; one of the proposed definitions of 'itq, considered divisible.
إثبات العتق (ithbat al-'itq): Affirmation of freedom; the alternative definition of 'itq, considered indivisible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the immediate legal status of a slave when his master emancipates half of him?
A) The slave becomes half free, half slave.
B) The slave becomes fully free at once.
C) Ownership of the half is removed, but the slave is still legally considered a slave.
D) The slave becomes a mukatab and must pay half his value.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over partial emancipation in the Hanafi school turns on whether 'itq is defined as divisible removal of ownership or indivisible affirmation of freedom, with the fatwa following the latter view.

Options for the Co-owner and the Condition of Wealth
Options for co-owner upon partial emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to a co-owner when a partner emancipates his share, and identify the condition of wealth required for the guarantee option.

Core Idea
A co-owner may free his share or demand compensation from a wealthy emancipator.

When you and your partner jointly own a slave, and one of you emancipates his share, you as the other co-owner have specific rights. The school outlines two clear options: you can free your own share, or if the emancipator is wealthy, you can oblige him to guarantee (daman) the value of your share. This lesson unpacks these options and the critical condition of wealth that determines which path is available.

Guided Path
1
Identify the co-owner's options
The text states that the partner (the non-emancipating co-owner) may either free his own share or require the emancipator to guarantee (daman) the value of his share. Recognize that these are the only two valid choices.
These options are specific to the co-owner, not the emancipator.
2
Note the condition of wealth
The guarantee option is only available if the emancipator is wealthy (maysir) — meaning he has enough wealth to cover the value of the partner's share. If he is poor, the partner only has the option to free his share.
Wealth is assessed at the time of emancipation.
3
Understand the effect on wala'
The wala' (clientage) belongs to both the partner and the original emancipator. This means both share in the inheritance rights from the freed slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال: بعضك حر أو جزء منك حر يؤمر بالبيان، ولو قال: سهم منك حر، عتق سدسه كما في الوصية بالسهم من عبده، وعندهما: يعتق كله في الكل. فيما بقي من قيمته، فإذا أدى عتق كله دفعة واحدة. وهو كالمكاتب عند أبي حنيفة له؛ لأنه كله رقيق وإنما زال الملك عن بعضه بإضافة العتق إليه فعمل بالدليلين بإنزاله مكاتباً حتى لا تقبل شهادته ولا يرث ولا يورث ولا يتزوج إلا أنه إذا عجز لا يرد إلى الرق؛ لأن المولى أوجب السعاية لوقوع الحرية في البعض، وهو موجود بعد العجز، بخلاف المكاتب كتابة مقصودة، وعندهما هو كالحر المديون؛ لأن العتق وقع في جميعه بناء على ما تقدم، وهو قول الشافعي له، ولا سعاية عليه. أن يحرر : إما أن يعتق نصيبه. أو يضمن أي المعتق قيمة نصيبه للشريك لو موسراً: أي لو كان المعتق غنياً بأن يكون مالكاً قدر قيمة نصيب الآخر. لهما، أي العبد والولاء له، ولو شهد كلٌّ بعتق نصيب صاحبه سعى لهما، أي للشريكين موسرين كانا أو معسرين أو مختلفين والولاء لهما. أي بعد أن يحلف كل منهما على دعوى صاحبه، سعى لكل واحد منهما في نصيبه؛ لأن كل واحد منهما يشهد على صاحبه بالعتق وعلى نفسه بالتكاتب، فلا يقبل قوله على صاحبه ويقبل في حق نفسه ويمتنع به استرقاقه، فيستسعيه للتيقن به؛ لأنه إن كان صادقاً فهو مكاتبه وإن كان كاذباً
And if he says: 'Some of you is free' or 'A part of you is free', he is ordered to specify. And if he says: 'A share of you is free', a sixth of him becomes free, as in the bequest of a share of a slave. According to the two companions, he becomes entirely free in all cases. Regarding what remains of his value, when he pays it, he becomes fully free at once. According to Abu Hanifa, he is like a mukatab because he is entirely a slave, only ownership has been removed from a part by attaching emancipation to it. So he treated him as a mukatab by combining both proofs, such that his testimony is not accepted, he does not inherit, is not inherited, and does not marry. However, if he becomes unable (to pay), he is not returned to slavery because the master made sa'y obligatory due to the occurrence of freedom in the part, which remains after inability. This differs from a mukatab with a contracted kitaba. According to the two companions, he is like a free debtor because emancipation occurred in all of him based on what preceded, and this is the view of al-Shafi'i, and there is no sa'y upon him. He (the partner) may either free his share or guarantee (daman) the value of his share to the partner if the emancipator is wealthy, i.e., if he owns enough to cover the value of the other's share. The wala' belongs to him (the emancipator) and to the partner. And if each of the two co-owners testifies that the other emancipated his share, the slave works (sa'y) for both, whether they are wealthy or poor or different, and the wala' belongs to both. That is, after each takes an oath on the other's claim, he works for each for his share, because each testifies against the other regarding emancipation and against himself regarding kitaba, so his statement is not accepted against the other but is accepted against himself, and it prevents his enslavement. Thus he is made to work for certainty, because if he is truthful, he is their mukatab, and if he is lying...
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage continues with details of the rulings. First, it discusses expressions of partial emancipation. If the master says 'Some of you is free' or 'A part of you is free', he must clarify what he meant. If he says 'A share of you is free', according to Abu Hanifa, a specific portion (one-sixth by analogy) becomes free, but according to the two companions, the whole becomes free. Then it explains that the slave must pay the remaining value; upon payment, he becomes free. Abu Hanifa likens him to a mukatab (a slave who contracts to purchase his freedom) because the slave is still considered a slave in legal matters. However, unlike a regular mukatab, if he fails to pay, he is not returned to slavery because the master imposed sa'y as a means to freedom, and the partial freedom is already effective. The two companions consider him a free man with debt, following the principle that partial emancipation results in full freedom. The passage then lists the options for the co-owner: he can either free his own share, or claim compensation (daman) from the wealthy emancipator for the value of his share. The wala' (clientage) goes to both the partner and the emancipator. A special case is when each co-owner testifies that the other freed his share. In that case, the slave must work for both, because each witness's testimony is accepted against himself but not against the other. The slave's status becomes uncertain, so he is made to work to ensure justice.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): A guarantee by the wealthy emancipator to pay the partner the value of his share.
ولاء (wala'): The clientage relationship that establishes inheritance rights between the emancipator and the freed slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The co-owner must be poor.
D) The emancipation must be in writing.

Key Takeaway
A co-owner's options upon a partner's emancipation are either to free his own share or demand compensation from a wealthy emancipator, with wala' shared.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Nature of the Slave after Partial Emancipation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on slave's status after partial emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare Abu Hanifa's view (the slave is like a mukatab) with the view of the two companions (the slave is a free debtor) and state the practical consequences.

Core Idea
After partial emancipation, Abu Hanifa treats the slave as a mukatab, the two companions as a free debtor.

When only part of a slave is freed, what is his status? Abu Hanifa likens him to a mukatab (a slave contracting for freedom), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad treat him as a free man with debt. This disagreement has real-world implications for the slave's ability to testify, inherit, marry, and what happens if he cannot pay.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core difference
Abu Hanifa holds the slave is entirely a slave except for the freed part, so he is like a mukatab. The two companions say the emancipation applies to all of him, making him a free debtor.
The difference stems from how the emancipatory words affect the whole.
2
List the consequences according to Abu Hanifa
The slave's testimony is not accepted, he does not inherit or bequeath, and he cannot marry. But if he fails to pay, he is not returned to slavery because partial freedom is already effective.
This is unlike a regular mukatab who can be returned if he fails.
3
List the consequences according to the two companions
The slave is essentially free, so he can testify, inherit, etc. He only owes the remaining value as a debt. No sa'y (earning) is required.
This view aligns with the Shafi'i school.
4
Analyze the testimony case
If each co-owner testifies that the other freed his share, the slave is made to work for both because each testimony is accepted against oneself but not against the other. This situation is resolved by making the slave work to satisfy both claims.

This passage continues with details of the rulings. First, it discusses expressions of partial emancipation. If the master says 'Some of you is free' or 'A part of you is free', he must clarify what he meant. If he says 'A share of you is free', according to Abu Hanifa, a specific portion (one-sixth by analogy) becomes free, but according to the two companions, the whole becomes free. Then it explains that the slave must pay the remaining value; upon payment, he becomes free. Abu Hanifa likens him to a mukatab (a slave who contracts to purchase his freedom) because the slave is still considered a slave in legal matters. However, unlike a regular mukatab, if he fails to pay, he is not returned to slavery because the master imposed sa'y as a means to freedom, and the partial freedom is already effective. The two companions consider him a free man with debt, following the principle that partial emancipation results in full freedom. The passage then lists the options for the co-owner: he can either free his own share, or claim compensation (daman) from the wealthy emancipator for the value of his share. The wala' (clientage) goes to both the partner and the emancipator. A special case is when each co-owner testifies that the other freed his share. In that case, the slave must work for both, because each witness's testimony is accepted against himself but not against the other. The slave's status becomes uncertain, so he is made to work to ensure justice.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master to earn his freedom by paying a sum.
سعاية (sa'y): Effort, earning; the work the slave must do to pay for his freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two companions (Sahibayn), what is the status of a slave after one co-owner emancipates his share?
A) He remains a slave until he pays the value.
B) He becomes free immediately but owes the remaining value as a debt.
C) He becomes a mukatab.
D) He is freed unconditionally with no debt.

Key Takeaway
After partial emancipation, Abu Hanifa views the slave as a mukatab with restrictions, while the two companions see him as free with debt, and the mufta bihi is the latter.
