Conditional Emancipation by Partners and Oaths on Separate Slaves
Rulings on conditional emancipation with opposite conditions and separate slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling when two partners suspend emancipation on opposite conditions for a jointly owned slave, analyze the Hanafi ikhtilaf on the slave's earning, and distinguish the scenario of separate slaves where consensus applies.

Core Idea
When conditions are opposite and outcome unknown, half a jointly owned slave is freed; for separate slaves, neither is freed due to extreme uncertainty.

This passage presents a nuanced fiqh problem: two partners each condition the emancipation of their jointly owned slave on opposite events, and the event's outcome is unknown. The Hanafi school agrees that half the slave is freed, but disputes the earning. A second scenario involving separate slaves leads to consensus that no emancipation occurs. Understanding the reasoning (ta'lil) behind each is key to mastering the principles of conditional emancipation and certainty (yaqin).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The first involves a jointly owned slave with opposite conditions; the second involves two separate slaves each with a condition on the same event. Recognize the key differences: joint ownership vs separate ownership, and the role of consensus.
Pay attention to the wording: 'ولو علّق أحدهما عتقه بفعل فلان غدا وعكس الآخر' vs 'ولو حلف كل واحد بعتق عبده'.
2
Analyze the first scenario: joint slave
Since the conditions are opposite, exactly one oath is broken. The slave becomes half-free with certainty. The remaining half must be compensated via earning (sa'y).
The certainty (yaqin) is that half the slave is freed; the compensation is wajib.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on sa'y
Abu Hanifa: slave earns half his value for both partners regardless of wealth. Abu Yusuf: wealth matters—if both wealthy, no earning; if one poor, quarter for the wealthy. Muhammad: slave earns full value because the one whose half-sa'y is dropped is unknown.
Mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view; it is the most precautionary.
4
Analyze the second scenario: separate slaves
Each man swears on his own slave with opposite conditions. The uncertainty is extreme: we do not know which slave should be freed, nor which master broke the oath. By consensus (ijma'), neither slave is freed.
The reasoning: here both beneficiary and subject are unknown, unlike the first scenario where the beneficiary (half-slave) is known.
5
Compare the reasoning
In the first scenario, the known (half-freedom) overcomes the unknown (which oath broke). In the second, the unknown dominates, so judgment cannot be made.
This illustrates the principle: certainty is not overridden by doubt (al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو علّق أحدهما عتقه بفعل فلان غداً وعكس الآخر ومضى ولم يدر عتق نصفه وسعى في نصفه لهما ولو حلف كل واحد بعتق عبده لم يعتق واحد إجماعاً، وعند الإمام: الولاء لهما، وعند الثلاثة لو حلفا أو نكلا، ففي عتق نصيب كل منهما قولان. فلان بأن قال: إن دخل زيد الدار غدا فأنت حر . وعكس الآخر بأن قال: إن لم يدخل زيد الدار غداً فأنت حر . ولم يدر: أي لم يعلم أدخل زيد الدار أم لا . عتق نصفه : أي نصف العبد للتيقن بحنث أحدهما. وسعى في نصفه لهما : أنصافاً مطلقاً سواء كان أو أحدهما موسراً والآخر معسراً عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن يسار المعتق لا يمنع وجوب السعاية على العبد عنده وعند أبي يوسف إن كانا معسرين سعى في نصف قيمته لهما، وإن كان موسرين لم يسع لواحد منهما في شيء؛ لأن كل واحد منهما يتبرأ من السعاية، الضمان على شريكه، فإن يسار المعتق يمنع وجوب السعاية عند أبي يوسف، وإن كان أحدهما موسراً والآخر معسراً سعي في ربع قيمته للموسر؛ لأن المعسر يدعي الضمان على شريكه، ويتبرأ من سعاية العبد فيسقط حقه والموسر يدعي السعاية على العبد، فيسعى له في حصته، فأبو يوسف مع الإمام في المقدار، ومع محمد في اعتبار اليسار والإعسار وعند محمد: يسعى في جميع قيمته؛ لأن المقضى عليه بسقوط نصف السعاية مجهول، فلا يمكن القضاء به مع الجهالة، ولهما: أنا تيقنا بحنث أحدهما وسقوط نصف السعاية عن العبد، فلا يجوز القضاء به مع التيقن بخلافه، وهذا فيما إذا كانا معسرين وإن كان موسرين لم يسع لواحد منهما في شيء، وإن كانا مختلفين يسعى في نصف قيمته للمعسر، ولم يسع للموسر. ولو حلف كل واحد بعتق عبده بأن كان لهما عبدان مخلفاً عليهما كل واحد منهما لأحدهما لم يعتق واحد إجماعاً؛ لأن الجهالة في المقضي له والمقضي عليه متفاحشة، فامتنع القضاء بخلاف المسألة السابقة في العبد الواحد فإن المقضي له بالحرية وسقوط نصف السعاية معلوم والمجهول واحد والحانث منهما، فغلب المعلوم المجهول، وفي هذه المسألة بالعكس؛ لأن المجهول هو الغالب في المقضي له والمقضي عليه فامتنع القضاء لذلك.
And if one of them suspends his emancipation upon the action of so-and-so tomorrow, and the other reverses it, and the time passes and it is not known, half of the slave is freed and he earns in half for them both. And if each of two men swears upon his own slave, neither is freed by consensus. According to the Imam, the wala' is for both. According to the three, if they swore or refused, there are two opinions regarding the emancipation of each's share. 'So-and-so' means he said: 'If Zayd enters the house tomorrow, you are free.' And the other reverses: 'If Zayd does not enter the house tomorrow, you are free.' 'And it is not known' means it is not known whether Zayd entered or not. 'Half of the slave is freed' means half of the slave due to certainty of one's oath being broken. 'And he earns in half for them both' means halves absolutely, whether both are wealthy or poor or one wealthy and one poor according to Abu Hanifa, because the wealth of the emancipator does not prevent the obligation of earning upon the slave according to him. According to Abu Yusuf, if both are poor, he earns half his value for both; if both are wealthy, he does not earn for either, because each disavows earning, the guarantee is upon his partner, for the wealth of the emancipator prevents the obligation of earning according to Abu Yusuf. If one is wealthy and the other poor, he earns one quarter of his value for the wealthy one, because the poor one claims guarantee upon his partner and disavows the slave's earning, so his right falls; the wealthy one claims earning from the slave, so he earns for his share. So Abu Yusuf agrees with the Imam in quantity, and with Muhammad in considering wealth and poverty. According to Muhammad, he earns the entire value, because the one judged to have half the earning dropped is unknown, so judgment cannot be made with ignorance. Their argument: we are certain that one oath is broken and half the earning is dropped from the slave, so judgment cannot be made contrary to certainty. This is when both are poor; if both are wealthy, he does not earn for either; if they differ, he earns half his value for the poor one and not for the wealthy one. And if each of two men swears upon his own slave, meaning they have two slaves, each belonging to one of them, neither is freed by consensus, because the ignorance regarding the beneficiary and the subject is extreme, so judgment is prevented, unlike the previous case of one slave, where the beneficiary of freedom and the dropping of half the earning are known, the unknown is one, and the oath-breaker is from them, so the known overcomes the unknown. In this case, the opposite, because the unknown dominates in both beneficiary and subject, so judgment is prevented for that reason.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents two separate rulings within the chapter on partial emancipation. First scenario: two partners each condition the emancipation of their jointly owned slave on opposite conditions regarding the same event (e.g., one says 'if Zayd enters tomorrow, you are free,' the other says 'if Zayd does not enter, you are free'). When the event's occurrence is unknown, one condition must have been triggered, so half the slave is free. The slave must then earn (sa'y) for both partners. There is an ikhtilaf among the Hanafi imams on the extent of earning. Abu Hanifa holds that earning is required in full half for both regardless of wealth. Abu Yusuf differentiates based on the partners' wealth: if both poor, slave earns half for both; if both wealthy, no earning; if one poor one wealthy, slave earns quarter for the wealthy (the poor's share is compensated via guarantee). Muhammad holds that the slave must earn the entire value because the one whose half-sa'y is dropped is unknown, so cannot be omitted. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa, as it is the most precautionary. Second scenario: two separate men each own a separate slave, and each makes a conditional oath on their own slave with opposite conditions (one says 'if Zayd enters, my slave is free,' the other says 'if Zayd does not enter, my slave is free'). Here, by consensus, neither slave is freed because the uncertainty is extreme: we do not know which man broke his oath, and each slave is separate, so no judgment can be made. The reasoning is that in the previous case of one slave, the beneficiary is known (the slave will be half free), but here both the beneficiary and the subject are unknown, so judgment is impossible.

Key Terms
تعليق العتق (ta‘līq al-‘itq): Suspending emancipation upon the occurrence of a future condition; the slave becomes free upon fulfillment.
حنث (ḥinth): The breaking of an oath; here, the occurrence of the condition that triggers the emancipation.
إجماع (ijmā‘): Consensus of the scholars; here, that neither slave is freed in the second scenario.
سعي (sa‘y): Earning; the compensation required from the slave for the part of his value not freed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario (jointly owned slave with opposite conditions), what is the ruling according to the mufta bihi position?
A) The slave is fully freed and no earning is required.
B) Half the slave is freed and the slave must earn half his value for both partners.
C) The slave remains in joint ownership because the conditions cancel out.
D) The slave becomes a mudabbar.

Key Takeaway
Conditional emancipation with opposite conditions yields half-freedom for a jointly owned slave, but no freedom for separate slaves due to consensus on extreme uncertainty.

Joint Owners' Opposite Conditional Manumission: The Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on joint owners' opposite conditional manumission with unknown event

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a joint owner's opposite conditional manumissions with unknown condition occurrence, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the slave's sa'ya (work) amount based on owners' solvency.

Core Idea
When two co-owners make opposite conditional manumissions and the condition is unknown, half the slave is freed; the slave works for half value according to Abu Hanifa regardless of solvency.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi manumission law: two joint owners each conditionally manumit a common slave with opposite conditions (e.g., one says 'if Zaid enters, you are free' and the other says 'if Zaid does not enter, you are free'). If the day passes and it is unknown whether the condition occurred, one of the owners must have violated his oath, but it is unclear which. The ruling is that half the slave is freed. However, the Hanafi imams differ on how much work (sa'ya) the slave must perform to compensate the owners for their loss.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the key legal question
The two co-owners each make a conditional manumission with opposite conditions, and the condition's occurrence is unknown. The question: how much work must the slave do (sa'ya) to compensate?
2
Establish the agreed-upon basis
All agree that half the slave is freed because one owner must have violated his condition (hanth). The slave must work for some value.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's position
He rules that the slave works for half the value absolutely, regardless of the owners' solvency. His reasoning: the right to sa'ya is not nullified by solvency.
4
Examine Abu Yusuf's differentiation
He distinguishes based on owners' financial status: if both are insolvent, slave works half; if both solvent, no work; if one solvent and one insolvent, slave works a quarter for the solvent. Reason: solvency of owner prevents sa'ya obligation.
5
Consider Muhammad's full-value position
He rules that the slave works for the full value because the identity of the owner for whom the work is dropped is unknown, so cannot be reduced. Evaluate the practical differences.

(مسكين)
The Book of Manumission. Section on the slave who is partially set free. And if one of them (two co-owners) attached his emancipation to the action of so-and-so tomorrow, and the other reversed it, and (the day) passed and it is not known (whether the condition occurred), half of him is freed and he works for half (his value) for both of them. And if each of two men swore by the emancipation of his slave, not one is freed by consensus. The slave (works) for half his value for the two partners, and the wala' belongs to both from the two men (i.e., the two owners) from the two slaves (i.e., the two slaves) by consensus. (If) they were both insolvent... it is his slave, and this does not differ by solvency or insolvency according to him (Abu Hanifa) because the right to seek work is not nullified by solvency. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If one of them is insolvent, (yes), not if both are solvent, and the wala' is suspended until they agree on manumitting one of them; Zufar said the same. He works whether they are solvent or insolvent. According to Imam (Abu Hanifa): the wala' belongs to both. According to the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar): if they swore or refused, then regarding the emancipation of the share of each, there are two opinions. (Ayni, Fath) 'So-and-so' as in: if Zaid enters the house tomorrow then you are free. (T) 'Tomorrow' is an adverb for the action of so-and-so as a metaphor, and the intent is a specific time. (Hashiya) 'And the other reversed it' meaning he said: if Zaid does not enter the house tomorrow then you are free. (Ayni) 'And he did not know' meaning he did not know whether Zaid entered or not. (Ayni) 'Half of him is freed' meaning half of the slave due to certainty of the violation of one of them. (T) 'And he works for half for both of them' absolutely in halves, whether both are solvent or one is solvent and the other insolvent according to Abu Hanifa, because the solvency of the manumitter does not prevent the obligation of work (sa'ya) on the slave according to him. According to Abu Yusuf: if both are insolvent, he works for half his value for both; if both are solvent, he does not work for either of them because each disclaims from the work, (placing) the guarantee on his partner, for the solvency of the manumitter prevents the obligation of work according to Abu Yusuf. If one is solvent and the other insolvent, he works for a quarter of his value for the solvent one (only); because the insolvent claims guarantee from his partner and disclaims from the slave's work, so his right falls; and the solvent claims work from the slave, so he works for his share. So Abu Yusuf agrees with the Imam in amount but disagrees with Muhammad regarding the consideration of solvency and insolvency. According to Muhammad: he works for his entire value, because the one against whom half the work was dropped is unknown, so it cannot be decreed with ignorance. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: we are certain of the violation of one of them and the dropping of half the work from the slave, so it is not permissible to decree (dropping) with certainty of the contrary. This is when both are insolvent; if both are solvent, he does not work for either; if they are different, he works for half his value for the insolvent and does not work for the solvent. (Miskin)
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi ruling on a scenario where two people jointly own a slave, and each makes a conditional statement regarding the slave's emancipation with opposite conditions (e.g., one says 'If Zaid enters the house tomorrow, you are free,' and the other says 'If Zaid does not enter, you are free'). If the day passes without knowing whether the condition occurred, one of the two owners must have been violated (hanth), but it is unknown which. Since the slave cannot be partially free in the sense of complete emancipation only, the ruling is that half the slave is freed (i.e., the share of the one who violated his condition), and the slave must work (sa'ya) to earn half his value to compensate the owners for the loss of their property. The passage then details a dispute among the Hanafi imams regarding the amount of work required based on the owners' financial status. Abu Hanifa holds that the slave works for half his value absolutely regardless of their solvency, because the right to seek work does not depend on the owner's wealth. Abu Yusuf differentiates: if both are insolvent, the slave works half; if both are solvent, no work is required; if one is solvent and one insolvent, the slave works a quarter for the solvent only. Muhammad says the slave works for the entire value, because the identity of the one whose half is dropped is unknown, and ignorance prevents the reduction. The passage also includes a brief note on the wala' (loyalty) of the freed portion: according to Abu Hanifa, the wala' belongs to both owners; according to the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar), it is suspended until they agree on emancipation. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute based on different applications of istihsan and qiyas.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): A conditional statement of emancipation, e.g., 'if Zaid enters, you are free'.
السعاية (al-sa'aya): Work performed by a partially freed slave to earn his value for the owner(s).
الولاء (al-wala'): The bond of patronage between the freed slave and the manumitter.
الحنث (al-hanth): Violation of a condition (e.g., the condition occurred).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He works for the full value.
B) He works for half the value regardless of the owners' wealth.
C) He works only if both owners are insolvent.
D) He does not work at all.

Key Takeaway
When opposite conditional manumissions make a common slave partially free, the slave compensates the owners through sa'ya (work), and the Hanafi imams split on the amount based on solvency and certainty.

Excessive Ignorance Prevents Conditional Emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between acceptable and excessive ignorance (jahalah mutafahishah) in conditional emancipation and state the ruling that neither slave is freed when both the violator and recipient are unknown.

Core Idea
When the recipient and violator are both unknown, the ignorance is excessive and the ruling of freedom is prevented.

This lesson continues from the previous scenario of joint ownership, now examining two separate slaves with opposite conditions. The key is understanding how the degree of ignorance (jahalah) affects the legal outcome—whether it is excessive or not, and how that determines the validity of the conditional emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the case involves two separate slaves, each owned by a different person, with opposite conditional statements about the same event (e.g., one says if Zayd enters, his slave is free; the other says if Zayd does not enter, his slave is free).
This is distinct from the previous case of a single slave co-owned by two partners.
2
Determine the unknown factors
After the event's deadline passes without knowledge, identify what remains unknown: which owner violated his condition (the hanith) and consequently which slave should be freed and from whom the work (sa'ya) is due.
Both the recipient (al-maqdi lahu) and the subject (al-maqdi 'alayhi) of the ruling are unknown.
3
Compare with the previous case
In the previous case of one co-owned slave, the recipient of freedom (the slave) and the amount of work dropped (half) were known; only the violator was unknown. Here, the unknown is more than the known.
This contrast highlights the principle that excessive ignorance prevents judicial determination.
4
Apply the principle
Since the unknown dominates, the ruling of emancipation is prevented by consensus (ijma'). Neither slave is freed.
The Hanafi principle is that excessive ignorance (jahalah mutafahishah) blocks the legal judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعتق عبده : بأن كان لهما عبدان مخلفاً عليهما كل واحد منهما لأحدهما كما في المسألة السابقة. (عيني) لم يعتق واحد: أي لو كان مكان الشريكين رجلان وقال أحدهما: إن دخل فلان الدار غداً فعبدي حر، وقال الآخر: إن لم يدخل فلان الدار غداً فعبدي حر فمضي الغد ولم يعلم دخل أم لا فلا يعتق واحد منهما إجماعاً؛ لأن الجهالة في المقضي له والمقضي عليه متفاحشة، فامتنع القضاء بخلاف المسألة السابقة في العبد الواحد فإن المقضي له بالحرية وسقوط نصف السعاية معلوم والمجهول واحد والحانث منهما، فغلب المعلوم المجهول، وفي هذه المسألة بالعكس؛ لأن المجهول هو الغالب في المقضي له والمقضي عليه فامتنع القضاء لذلك، ولا يشكل هذا بما إذا كان بينهما عبد وأمة، فقال أحدهما إن دخل فلان الدار اليوم فالعبد حر، وقال الآخر: إن لم يدخل فالأمة حرة، =
By 'the emancipation of his slave' means that they had two slaves, each one belonging to one of them as in the previous case. (Ayni) 'Not one is freed' means: if instead of two partners there were two men, and one said 'If so-and-so enters the house tomorrow, my slave is free,' and the other said 'If so-and-so does not enter the house tomorrow, my slave is free,' and the morrow passed and it is not known whether he entered or not, then neither slave is freed by consensus because the ignorance concerning the recipient and the subject (of the ruling) is excessive, so the judgment is prevented, unlike the previous case of one slave where the recipient of freedom and the dropping of half the work are known, and the unknown is only one (the violator), so the known overrides the unknown. In this case it is the opposite because the unknown is dominant in the recipient and the subject, so the judgment is prevented. This is not invalidated by the case where they have between them a male and a female slave, and one said 'If so-and-so enters the house today, the male slave is free,' and the other said 'If he does not enter, the female slave is free,' (then both are freed).
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage continues from the previous scenario but now deals with two distinct slaves, each owned by a different person. Each owner makes a conditional statement: one says if Zayd enters, his slave is free; the other says if Zayd does not enter, his slave is free. When the day passes without knowledge of the event, the question arises whether either slave becomes free. The ruling, by scholarly consensus (ijma'), is that neither slave is freed. The reason is that the ignorance (jahl) here is excessive: we do not know which owner violated his condition (the violator or hanith), and consequently we do not know which slave should be freed and from whom the work (sa'ya) is due. In the previous case of one slave co-owned, the recipient of freedom (the slave) and the amount of work dropped (half) were known, and only the identity of the violator was unknown. Here, both the recipient and the violator are unknown, leading to a 'fahisha' (excessive) ignorance that prevents judicial determination. The commentator distinguishes this from a similar case where two persons own a male and a female slave jointly and make opposite conditions; in that case, both slaves are freed (as discussed in the next lesson). This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that ignorance that dominates the judgment prevents it.

Key Terms
الجهالة المتفاحشة (al-jahalah al-mutafahishah): Excessive ignorance where the unknown elements are more than the known, preventing a legal ruling.
المقضي له والمقضي عليه (al-maqdi lahu wal-maqdi 'alayhi): The person for whom the ruling is made (recipient) and the person against whom it is made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are neither of the two slaves freed in the case where each of two men own a different slave and make opposite conditional statements?
A) Because the conditions are invalid.
B) Because the identity of the violator and the recipient of freedom are both unknown, causing excessive ignorance.
C) Because the owners refused to free them.
D) Because the slaves are not jointly owned.

Key Takeaway
In conditional emancipation, if the identity of the violator and the recipient of freedom are both unknown, the ignorance is excessive and the slave is not freed.

Both Slaves Freed: Opposite Conditions and Iqrar
Ruling when two men own a male and a female slave and make opposite conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two owners make opposite conditional emancipation statements and explain the role of iqrar (admission) in reaching that ruling.

Core Idea
When two owners admit each other's slave is free, both are freed — the admission binds regardless of unknown violator.

This passage presents a nuanced scenario where two co-owners of a male and a female slave each make a conditional statement that, if both were taken literally, would create a contradiction. Yet the Hanafi school rules that both slaves become free. The key lies in each owner's admission (iqrar) that his own claim is false and his partner's claim is true. Understanding this principle helps you see how Hanafi fiqh prioritises legal admissions over unknown facts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two people jointly own a male slave and a female slave. One says: 'If Zayd enters today, the male slave is free.' The other says: 'If Zayd does not enter, the female slave is free.'
The contradictory conditions mean exactly one condition is violated, but it is unknown which owner violated his condition.
2
Note the ruling
The Hanafi ruling is that both slaves are freed, even though the violator is unknown.
The ruling is based on each owner's admission (iqrar) that his own share is invalid because he believes his partner is the violator.
3
Analyse the reasoning
Each owner, by making his condition, effectively claims the other is at fault. In Hanafi law, such a claim is treated as an admission that the other's slave is free, leading to the freedom of both.
This differs from a previous case where each owner claimed ownership of the same slave, leading to a different result.
4
Consider the subcases
If the owners exchange the slaves, both become free and the sale is invalid because they sold free persons. If a third party buys both, the purchase is valid and one slave becomes free on the buyer.
These subcases show the practical consequences of the initial iqrar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان بينهما عبد وأمة، فقال أحدهما إن دخل فلان الدار اليوم فالعبد حر، وقال الآخر: إن لم يدخل فالأمة حرة، يعتق كل واحد منهما مع أن المقضي له بالعتق والمقضي عليه مجهول؛ لأن كل واحد منهما أقر بفساد نصيبه لزعمه أن شريكه هو الحانث، بخلاف السابقة، فإن كلاً منهما يزعم أن الآخر هو في عبده، وليس له فيه نصيب حتى لو تقايضا عتق عليهما؛ لإقرار كل منهما بحرية عبد الآخر، وعلى كل منهما قيمة ما اشترى؛ لأن كلاً . أنه اشترى حراً بعبد، ففسد البيع بإقرارهما، ولو اشترى العبدين في مسألتنا يزعم رجل واحد جاز وإن كان عالماً بحنث أحد البائعين؛ لزعم كل منهما أنه باع عبداً ، وزعم المشتري قبل الدخول في ملكه غير معتبر، فإذا صح الشراء واجتمعا في ملكه عتق عليه أحدهما؛ لأن زعمه معتبر في حق نفسه ويؤمر بالبيان رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۷/۱] (مستخلص) منهما .
And if there is between them a male slave and a female slave, and one of them says 'If so-and-so enters the house today, the male slave is free,' and the other says 'If he does not enter, the female slave is free,' both of them are freed, even though the recipient of the freedom and the subject are unknown, because each one of them admits the invalidity of his own share, believing that his partner is the violator, unlike the previous case where each believes that the other (owns) his slave and has no right in it. So if they exchange (taqayada), both become free on them, due to each admitting the freedom of the other's slave, and each must pay the value of what he bought, because each (bought) a free person with a slave, so the sale is invalid by their admission. And if a single person buys both slaves in our case, it is permissible even if he knows of one of the sellers' violation, because each seller claims he sold a slave, and the buyer's claim before entering into ownership is not considered. So if the purchase is valid and they come into his ownership, one of them becomes free on him, because his claim is valid regarding himself, and he is commanded to indicate (which one). (Reference to Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/327, Mustakhlas) from them.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents a third scenario: two persons jointly own a male slave and a female slave (or each owns one? The phrase 'between them' suggests joint ownership or each owning one slave). One owner makes a condition that if Zayd enters today, the male slave is free; the other makes a condition that if Zayd does not enter, the female slave is free. The ruling is that both slaves are freed. Despite the ignorance of which owner violated his condition, each owner effectively admits that the other's slave is free because each believes the other is the violator (hanith). This is different from the previous case where each owner believed the other owned the same slave. The passage also discusses what happens if the owners exchange the slaves (i.e., each buys the other's slave): both slaves become free on both owners (sellers) because each admits the other's slave is free, and each must pay the price of the slave he bought since the sale is invalid (they sold a free person). Furthermore, if a single buyer purchases both slaves with knowledge of one seller's violation, the purchase is valid because the sellers' claims of ownership are not contradicted by the buyer's knowledge before ownership. After purchase, one of the slaves becomes free on the buyer (since he cannot own both fully), and the buyer must clarify which one he intended to free. This shows the Hanafi principle of 'zann' (opinion) in contractual matters.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Admission or acknowledgment; here, each owner admits the other's slave is free.
التقايض (al-taqayud): Exchange of property, here the owners exchange the two slaves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling when two men jointly own a male and a female slave and make opposite conditional statements?
A) Only the male slave is freed.
B) Only the female slave is freed.
C) Both slaves are freed.
D) Neither slave is freed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, mutual admission (iqrar) by both owners leads to the freedom of both slaves, despite unknown violation.

Ruling on Co-ownership of a Mahram Relative: Guarantee and Option
Rulings on joint ownership of a relative and a stranger's purchase of half

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on guarantee and option when a person jointly owns a mahram relative with a partner, and distinguish Abu Hanifa's view from that of the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Acquiring a share of a mahram relative frees that share; compensation depends on who initiated the co-ownership.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses two related scenarios: one where a person co-owns his son (or other mahram) with a stranger, and another where a stranger buys half from the son and then the father buys the other half. The central dispute is whether the owner must compensate the co-owner for the freed share. Abu Hanifa holds that no compensation is due because the partner consented to the cause of emancipation, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require compensation if the owner is solvent. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for fatwa in property matters involving close relatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
The text presents two cases: (a) joint ownership of a mahram relative with a partner, (b) stranger buys half from the relative, then the other relative buys the remaining half.
The first scenario involves direct co-ownership; the second involves sequential purchases.
2
State the agreed ruling
In both cases, the share of the mahram relative is freed immediately upon acquisition, regardless of the other party's knowledge or solvency.
Freedom applies to the share, not the whole slave, but it is effective.
3
Analyze the dispute on guarantee
Abu Hanifa: no guarantee; the partner consented. Sahibayn: guarantee half value if solvent; if insolvent, the slave must work.
Abu Hanifa's argument: partner consented to the cause of emancipation.
4
Apply to the second scenario
Stranger has option to claim from father or demand work from son. Abu Hanifa gives choice; Sahibayn say father must guarantee.
The difference in the second scenario mirrors the first.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's: no guarantee for the owner, and option for the stranger.
This is the position for fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ملك ابنه مع آخر عتق حظه ولم يضمن ولشريكه أن يعتق أو يستسعي، وإن رجل أو أخاه اشترى نصفه أجنبي ثم الأب ما بقي فله أن يضمن الأب. الابن أي اشتري الأب من الابن وهو نصف الآخر أي شريك الأب الأب. نصيبه إن شاء العبد في قيمة نصيبه الحانث ولم يعرف أدخل أم لا، يعتق كل واحد منهما مع أن المقضي له بالعتق والمقضي عليه مجهول؛ لأن كل واحد منهما أقر بفساد نصيبه لزعمه أن شريكه هو الحانث، بخلاف السابقة، فإن كلاً منهما يزعم أن الآخر هو في عبده، وليس له فيه نصيب حتى لو تقايضا عتق عليهما؛ لإقرار كل منهما بحرية عبد الآخر، وعلى كل منهما قيمة ما اشترى؛ لأن كلاً . أنه اشترى حراً بعبد، ففسد البيع بإقرارهما، ولو اشترى العبدين في مسألتنا يزعم رجل واحد جاز وإن كان عالماً بحنث أحد البائعين؛ لزعم كل منهما أنه باع عبداً ، وزعم المشتري قبل الدخول في ملكه غير معتبر، فإذا صح الشراء واجتمعا في ملكه عتق عليه أحدهما؛ لأن زعمه معتبر في حق نفسه ويؤمر بالبيان رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۷/۱] (مستخلص) منهما . ولو ملك ابنه: فكذا الحكم في كل ذي رحم محرم سواء كان التملك بإرث بأن تزوج أمة ابن عمه فولدت ولداً ثم مات سيدها فورثه زوجها وابن عم آخر له أو بشراء أو بهبة أو بوصية أو بصدقة أو بإمهار، وقوله: مع آخر، أي مع رجل آخر بعقد واحد قبلاه جميعاً، وقوله: "عتق حظه"؛ لأنه ملك شقص قريبه فيعتق عليه، والمراد من قوله: "عتق" "حظه ثبوت أثره وزوال الملك، ولم يرد به حقيقة العتق؛ لأنه لا يتجزأ بالاتفاق، وقوله: "لم يضمن الأب" سواء كان موسراً أو معسراً، وسواء علم الآخر أنه ابن شريكه أو لم يعلم، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وعندهما يضمن الأب في غير الإرث نصف قيمته إن كان موسرا وإن كان معسرا سعى الابن في نصف قيمته لشريك أبيه، وعلى هذا الخلاف لو حلف أحدهما بعتق عبده إن ملك نصفه تملكاه بهذه الأسباب، لهما: أنه أفسد نصيبه بالإعتاق؛ لأن مباشرة هذه الأسباب إعتاق له ولهذا يجزئ به عن الكفارة، بخلاف ما إذا ورثاه؛ لأن الإرث جبري لا اختيار له فيه وله : أن الشريك رضي بإفساد نصيبه حيث شاركه في علة العتق، وهو مباشرة أسبابه، وعند الثلاثة: لا يعتق بشيء. (عيني، فتح) آخر: أي مع رجل آخر بالشراء أو الصدقة أو الهبة أو الوصية أو الإمهار أو الإرث. (عيني) عتق نصيب الأب بملك ذي رحم محرم علم الآخر أنه ابنه أو لم يعلم. (عيني) ولم يضمن أي العبد نصيب شريكه لانعدام التعدي فيه منه. (عيني) فله أن يضمن الأب من التضمين والأب مفعول، أي يأخذ الضمان الأب إن شاء. (عيني)] أي وإن ابتدأ الأجنبي فاشترى نصف الابن ثم اشترى الأب نصفه الآخر، وهو موسر، فالأجنبي بالخيار إن الأب؛ لأنه ما رضي بإفساد نصيبه حيث لم يشاركه في علة العتق وإن شاء استسعى الابن لاحتباس ماليته عنده وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا : لا خيار للأجنبي، ويضمن للأب نصف قيمته بناء على أن اليسار يمنع وجوب السعاية عندهما كما مر، فالضمان بالاتفاق، والاختلاف في الخيار. (مستخلص، فتح) مع شاء ضمن من حظه: أي
And if he owned his son (as a slave) together with another (co-owner), his share is freed and he does not guarantee (compensate), and his co-owner may either free (his share) or seek work (from the slave). And if a man (the son) or his brother bought half of him (the slave from the son?), then a stranger (bought the other half), then the father (bought) the remainder, so he (the stranger) has the option to claim (damages) from the father. (Commentary:) 'The son' means the father bought from the son, and it is the other half, i.e., the partner of the father (the son) is the father. ... (continues) 'And if he owned his son' (the ruling) is the same for every blood relative within the prohibited degrees, whether the ownership is by inheritance (e.g., he married a slave woman of his cousin, she gave birth, then her master died, and her husband inherited her along with another cousin), or by purchase, gift, bequest, charity, or dower. His statement 'with another' means with another man in a single contract both accepted. His statement 'his share is freed' means because he owned a share of his relative, so it becomes free for him. The meaning of 'his share is freed' is the establishment of its effect and removal of ownership, not the reality of emancipation because it is indivisible by agreement. His statement 'he does not guarantee the father' regardless of whether he is solvent or insolvent, and whether the partner knows he is his son or not — this is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the father guarantees half its value if he is solvent (in cases other than inheritance), and if insolvent the son works for half its value for his father's partner. Similarly, if one of them swore by emancipation (conditional on acquiring half) and they acquired it through these means, they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that he corrupted his share by emancipation, because directly engaging in these means constitutes emancipation and thus suffices for expiation (kaffarah), unlike inheritance because inheritance is compulsory with no choice. He (Abu Hanifa) says: the partner consented to the corruption of his share by participating in the cause of emancipation, i.e., directly engaging in its means. According to the three (Abu Hanifa's view? actually 'عند الثلاثة' likely refers to Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar): nothing is freed. (Ayni, Fath) 'with another' means with another man by purchase, charity, gift, bequest, dower, or inheritance. (Ayni) 'The share of the father is freed by owning a blood relative (mahram)' whether the other knows he is his son or not. (Ayni) 'And he does not guarantee' meaning the slave does not guarantee his partner's share because he has not committed any transgression. (Ayni) 'So he may guarantee the father' — from 'tadmeen' and the father is the object, i.e., he takes the guarantee from the father if he wishes. (Ayni) That is, if the stranger initiates by purchasing half from the son, then the father purchases the other half (while solvent), then the stranger has the choice: either he guarantees the father because the father did not consent to corruption of his share since he did not participate in the cause of emancipation, or he seeks work from the son because his wealth is retained by him. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: the stranger has no choice; the father must guarantee half its value, based on the principle that solvency prevents obligatory work (sa'ya) according to them, as previously. So guarantee is agreed upon, but the difference is over the choice. (Mustakhlas, Fath) 'With' (?) 'he wished' (?) 'he guaranteed' (?) 'from his share' meaning...
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage from page 13 deals with two related but distinct scenarios. First: if a person co-owns a close relative (like a son, brother, etc.) with another person, upon acquiring that relative's share, that share is freed immediately (a concept called 'surf' of freedom due to kinship) without any compensation to the other owner. The co-owner can then either free his own share or demand the slave to work (sa'ya) for the value. This applies whether the ownership is by inheritance, purchase, gift, etc., as per Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: if the owner is solvent, he must guarantee half the value to the co-owner; if insolvent, the son (the slave) works for half his value. Second scenario: A stranger buys half of the slave from the son (the slave), then the father buys the remaining half from the son. In this case, the stranger has an option: either he can claim damages from the father (since the father effectively caused the loss by taking the other half) or he can demand work from the son. Abu Hanifa allows this choice; the two scholars say the stranger has no choice, and the father must guarantee half the value because solvency of the father prevents sa'ya. The passage also clarifies that the ruling applies to all prohibited relatives (mahram), and that 'freedom' here means effect of removal of ownership, not complete emancipation of the whole slave (since that would be indivisible). The disputes are intra-Hanafi with implications for practical fatwa.

Key Terms
ذو الرحم المحرم (dhu al-rahim al-mahram): A close blood relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited (e.g., son, brother, father).
الضمان (al-daman): Guarantee or compensation, here the value of the co-owner's share.
الخيار (al-khiyar): Option or choice, here the stranger has the option to guarantee the father or seek work from the son.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a person co-owns his son as a slave with a partner, what is the ruling on the owner's share?
A) The owner's share is freed and he must compensate the partner.
B) The owner's share is freed without any compensation.
C) The owner must free the entire slave.
D) The slave becomes full owner of himself.

Key Takeaway
The share of a mahram relative is freed upon acquisition; Abu Hanifa holds no guarantee is due because the partner consented to the cause of emancipation.

Father Buying His Son with Another: Automatic Manumission and Guarantee
Ruling on father's joint ownership of son and partner's rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a father's joint ownership of his son and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding guarantee to the partner.

Core Idea
A father's ownership of his son frees his share automatically; guarantee of the partner's share is disputed.

This lesson explores a specific case in Hanafi fiqh: a father purchases his own son (a slave) jointly with an unrelated person. The father's share of the slave is automatically manumitted due to the close kinship (dhu rahim mahram). However, what happens to the partner's share? Is the father liable? The imams differ, and we will examine the proofs and the resulting practical differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
From the passage, state what happens when a father owns his son jointly with another person: the father's share is freed ( i.e., its legal effect takes place) without any guarantee.
The father's share is considered to have 'freed' in a legal sense, not actual partial freedom.
2
Understand the reason (ta'lil) for automatic manumission
The father is a mahram (close relative), and ownership of such a relative triggers manumission by istihsan. The slave has a right to freedom from near kinship.
This is based on the principle of removing the slave from ownership by a mahram, analogous to a father owning his child.
3
Analyze the dispute over guarantee (daman)
Contrast Abu Hanifa's view (no guarantee) with that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (guarantee half the value if wealthy, else the slave works). Identify the proof for each view.
Abu Hanifa uses istihsan: the partner consented to the joint purchase, thus accepted the risk.
4
Explore the cross-madhhab difference
According to al-Shafi'i (the Three), no manumission occurs at all because the ownership is not complete (the father owns only a share).
Hanafis argue that the effect of owning a mahram relative is immediate manumission of the share, and the slave becomes fully free after compensating the partner.
5
Apply the ruling to a similar scenario
Consider a case where a brother owns his brother, or a paternal uncle owns his nephew. The same principle applies: the mahram share frees, and the partner may claim compensation.
The passage extends the ruling to 'every close mahram relative' (dhū raḥim maḥram).

Source Text

ولو ملك ابنه مع آخر عتق حظه ولم يضمن ولشريكه أن يعتق أو يستسعي، وإن رجل أو أخاه اشترى نصفه أجنبي ثم الأب ما بقي فله أن يضمن الابن أي اشتري الأب من الابن وهو نصف الآخر أي شريك الأب الأب. نصيبه إن شاء العبد في قيمة نصيبه الحانث ولم يعرف أدخل أم لا، يعتق كل واحد منهما مع أن المقضي له بالعتق والمقضي عليه مجهول؛ لأن كل واحد منهما أقر بفساد نصيبه لزعمه أن شريكه هو الحانث، بخلاف السابقة، فإن كلاً منهما يزعم أن الآخر هو في عبده، وليس له فيه نصيب حتى لو تقايضا عتق عليهما؛ لإقرار كل منهما بحرية عبد الآخر، وعلى كل منهما قيمة ما اشترى؛ لأن كلاً . أنه اشترى حراً بعبد، ففسد البيع بإقرارهما، ولو اشترى العبدين في مسألتنا يزعم رجل واحد جاز وإن كان عالماً بحنث أحد البائعين؛ لزعم كل منهما أنه باع عبداً ، وزعم المشتري قبل الدخول في ملكه غير معتبر، فإذا صح الشراء واجتمعا في ملكه عتق عليه أحدهما؛ لأن زعمه معتبر في حق نفسه ويؤمر بالبيان رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۷/۱] (مستخلص) منهما . ولو ملك ابنه: فكذا الحكم في كل ذي رحم محرم سواء كان التملك بإرث بأن تزوج أمة ابن عمه فولدت ولداً ثم مات سيدها فورثه زوجها وابن عم آخر له أو بشراء أو بهبة أو بوصية أو بصدقة أو بإمهار، وقوله: مع آخر، أي مع رجل آخر بعقد واحد قبلاه جميعاً، وقوله: "عتق حظه"؛ لأنه ملك شقص قريبه فيعتق عليه، والمراد من قوله: "عتق" "حظه ثبوت أثره وزوال الملك، ولم يرد به حقيقة العتق؛ لأنه لا يتجزأ بالاتفاق، وقوله: "لم يضمن الأب" سواء كان موسراً أو معسراً، وسواء علم الآخر أنه ابن شريكه أو لم يعلم، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وعندهما يضمن الأب في غير الإرث نصف قيمته إن كان موسرا وإن كان معسرا سعى الابن في نصف قيمته لشريك أبيه، وعلى هذا الخلاف لو حلف أحدهما بعتق عبده إن ملك نصفه تملكاه بهذه الأسباب، لهما: أنه أفسد نصيبه بالإعتاق؛ لأن مباشرة هذه الأسباب إعتاق له ولهذا يجزئ به عن الكفارة، بخلاف ما إذا ورثاه؛ لأن الإرث جبري لا اختيار له فيه وله : أن الشريك رضي بإفساد نصيبه حيث شاركه في علة العتق، وهو مباشرة أسبابه، وعند الثلاثة: لا يعتق بشيء. (عيني، فتح) آخر: أي مع رجل آخر بالشراء أو الصدقة أو الهبة أو الوصية أو الإمهار أو الإرث. (عيني) عتق نصيب الأب بملك ذي رحم محرم علم الآخر أنه ابنه أو لم يعلم. (عيني) ولم يضمن أي العبد نصيب شريكه لانعدام التعدي فيه منه. (عيني) فله أن يضمن الأب من التضمين والأب مفعول، أي يأخذ الضمان الأب إن شاء. (عيني)]
And if he owns his son along with another, his share is freed and he does not guarantee, and for his partner he may free or seek earnings. And if a man or his brother bought half of a stranger, then the father what remained, then he may guarantee the son...
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on a father who jointly owns his son (a slave) with another person. The father's share automatically frees upon ownership due to the relationship of mahram. The father is not liable to compensate the partner, according to Abu Hanifa. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Two) hold that the father must guarantee half the value if he is wealthy; if poor, the son must work to compensate the partner. The discussion also covers a scenario where two people swear about manumission, and the view of al-Shafi'i (the Three) that no manumission occurs. The commentary clarifies that 'freeing of his share' means the legal effect, not actual freedom, as freedom cannot be partial.

Key Terms
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahim mahram): A close male relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited (e.g., son, father, brother).
حظه (hazzuhu): The share or portion of the slave owned by the relative.
إستسعاء (istis'ā'): Seeking earnings; the slave is required to work to pay off the partner's share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens when a father buys his son (a slave) jointly with an unrelated person?
A) The father's share frees, and he must pay the partner half the value.
B) The father's share frees, and he owes nothing to the partner.
C) The entire slave is freed immediately at the father's expense.
D) The father does not own his son, so no manumission occurs.

Key Takeaway
A father's ownership of a share in his son (a slave) triggers automatic manumission of that share, but the partner's rights are preserved through istis'ā' or, in the view of the Two, through a guarantee from the father.

Outsider Buys Half Then Father Buys Half
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khiyar when father buys his son's share.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two positions (Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn) regarding the stranger's khiyar in the case of a father buying his son's share, and explain the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
The stranger who bought half of a slave-son may choose to claim from the father or from the slave—Abu Hanifa grants the option; the Sahibayn deny it.

This passage examines a scenario where an outsider buys half of a slave who happens to be the son of another man, and then the father purchases the remaining half while being wealthy. The key legal question is whether the outsider has the right to choose between demanding compensation from the father or having the slave earn the value of his share. This lesson will walk you through the intra-Hanafi dispute, the underlying reasoning (talil), and the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of each view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and actions
First, identify who the stranger (ajnabi) is, what he bought (half of the slave-son), and what the father later did (bought the other half). Note that the father is wealthy (yasar).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa gives the stranger a khiyar (option): he may either demand compensation from the father, or let the slave earn (sa'y) the value of his half. The reason: the stranger did not consent to the father's action that led to the freeing.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the stranger has no option; the father must pay half the value directly. Their basis: the father's wealth (yasar) prevents the need for the slave to earn, so liability falls on the father.
4
Analyze the reasoning difference
Compare the two reasonings: Abu Hanifa focuses on the stranger's consent (or lack thereof) as the illa for granting an option; the Sahibayn prioritize the father's wealth as the determinant of liability, thus eliminating the slave's earning obligation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the stranger can choose the easier or more reliable source of payment; under Sahibayn, the father is automatically liable, and the stranger has no choice.
The fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view, so the stranger does have the option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي وإن ابتدأ الأجنبي فاشترى نصف الابن ثم اشترى الأب نصفه الآخر، وهو موسر، فالأجنبي بالخيار إن الأب؛ لأنه ما رضي بإفساد نصيبه حيث لم يشاركه في علة العتق وإن شاء استسعى الابن لاحتباس ماليته عنده وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وقالا : لا خيار للأجنبي، ويضمن للأب نصف قيمته بناء على أن اليسار يمنع وجوب السعاية عندهما كما مر، فالضمان بالاتفاق، والاختلاف في الخيار. (مستخلص، فتح)
Meaning: if a stranger initiates and buys half of the son, then the father buys the other half while he is wealthy, then the stranger has the option: if he wishes he may take guarantee from the father, because the father did not accept the damage to his share as he did not participate in the cause of freeing, and if he wishes he may seek earnings from the son, because his wealth is detained with him. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the Two said: the stranger has no option, and the father must guarantee half his value, based on the principle that wealth prevents the necessity of earning, as has passed. So the guarantee is agreed upon, and the difference is about the option.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where an unrelated person (ajnabi) first buys half of a slave who is the son of another man, and then the father buys the remaining half. The father is wealthy. According to Abu Hanifa, the stranger has a choice: either he can demand compensation from the father (because the father damaged the stranger's share by his purchase that caused freeing), or he can let the slave earn his half's value. This is because the father did not originally share in the cause of freeing. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the stranger has no choice; the father must pay half the value because wealth prevents the need for the slave to earn. The dispute is only about the existence of the option, not about the guarantee itself.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): Option or choice available to the injured party to seek compensation from either the father or the slave.
الحانث (al-hanith): The one who broke his oath; in context, the partner who is deemed to have caused the freeing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what option does the stranger have when the father buys the other half of his son?
A) He must demand payment from the father only.
B) He may choose to demand from the father or let the slave earn.
C) He must free the slave himself.
D) He has no option; the father pays automatically.

Key Takeaway
In this intra-Hanafi dispute, Abu Hanifa grants the stranger an option to claim from the father or the slave, while the Sahibayn hold the father is directly liable; the fatwa follows Abu Hanifa.

Father Buying Half of Son: Abu Hanifa's No-Guarantee Ruling vs. Sahibayn
Ruling on father buying half of son from sole owner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's position on the guarantee when a father buys half of his son from a full owner, explain his reasoning (talil), and compare it with the Sahibayn's view.

Core Idea
When a father buys half his son, the seller's participation in the sale cancels the guarantee (Abu Hanifa).

This passage examines a specific case of 'itq (freeing) and daman (guarantee). When a father purchases half of his son from a seller who owned the entire son, does the father owe any compensation to the seller? Abu Hanifa says no, because the seller himself shared in the cause of the freeing. The Two Imams (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, requiring guarantee if the father is wealthy. Understanding this dispute reveals the Hanafi approach to causation in freeing slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the intra-Hanafi dispute
The case: a father buys half of his son from a seller who owned the whole son. The dispute: does the father owe guarantee (daman) to the seller?
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that the seller participated in the cause ('illa) of the freeing by selling, so no guarantee is due. The seller is not entitled to compensation because he was a partner in the act that led to the freeing.
The 'illa here is the sale itself, which contributed to the freeing.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that if the father is wealthy (mūsir), he must pay guarantee to the seller. They see the father's purchase with wealth as the trigger for liability.
Wealth is the key condition for the Sahibayn.
4
Note the consensus when buying from one of two partners
If the father buys half from one of two co-owners, guarantee is obligatory by consensus if he is wealthy. This contrasts with the case of a sole owner.
This shows that the dispute is limited to the case where the seller owned the whole son.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and the practical consequence
The fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view. Thus, in practice, a father buying half from a full owner does not owe guarantee. The difference between the two positions affects whether the father has to pay.
Al-Marghinani prefers Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو يستسعي، وإن اشترى نصف ابنه ممن يملك كله لا يضمن لبائعه. عبد الموسرين أي الأب أي كل الابن أي الأب شيئاً عند أبي حنيفة دبره واحد وحرّر آخر ضمّن الساكتُ المدبّر، والمدير المعتق ثلثه مدبّراً لا منهم أولا منهم أي ثلث قيمته ما ضمن أو يستسعي: أي الابن في قيمة نصيبه أي النصف بوجود التعدي من الأب. (عيني) يضمن لبائعه: أي إذا اشتري رجل نصف الابن من البائع الذي كان مالكاً لكل الابن لا يضمن الأب لبائعه شيئاً عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن البائع شاركه في العلة وهو البيع، وقالا : إن كان الأب موسراً يجب عليه الضمان ولو اشتراه أبوه من أحد الشريكين، وهو موسر لزمه الضمان بالإجماع، ولو كان مكان الابن جارية مستولدة بالنكاح فملكها الزوج مع غيره يجب عليه ضمان النصف لشريكه كيف ما كان وإن كانا ملكاها بإرث . رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۸/۱] (مستخلص)
Or he may seek earnings. And if he buys half of his son from one who owns the whole, he does not guarantee to his seller. The servant of the wealthy... or he may seek earnings: meaning the son in the value of his share i.e. half due to the transgression of the father. (Ayni) He guarantees to his seller: meaning if a man buys half of the son from a seller who owned all the son, the father does not guarantee anything to his seller according to Abu Hanifa; because the seller shared in the cause, which is the sale. And the Two said: if the father is wealthy, he must guarantee. And if the father buys it from one of the two partners, and he is wealthy, the guarantee is obligatory by consensus. And if instead of the son there was an umm walad by marriage, and the husband owns her with another, he must guarantee half to his partner in all cases, even if they inherited her. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 328/1) (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where a father buys half of his son from a seller who owned the entire son. According to Abu Hanifa, the father does not owe any guarantee to the seller, because the seller participated in the cause of the freeing (i.e., by selling, he contributed to the situation). However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say that if the father is wealthy, he must pay guarantee. If the father buys from one of two co-owners, guarantee is due by consensus if wealthy. The passage also mentions an analogous case of an umm walad married to someone; if the husband owns her with another, he must guarantee half to his partner regardless of how they came to own her.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Guarantee or liability to pay compensation for the value of the freed share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does the father not guarantee the seller when buying half of his son from a full owner?
A) Because the seller acted wrongfully.
B) Because the seller participated in the cause of the freeing by selling.
C) Because the son is not a slave.
D) Because the father has no wealth.

Key Takeaway
A father buying half of his son from a sole owner is not liable for guarantee under Abu Hanifa because the seller shared in the cause of freeing.

Three Masters: Mudabbar and Manumitter
Ruling on slave owned by three, one makes mudabbar, one frees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the divisibility of tadbir and calculate the compensation among partners when one master makes a slave mudabbar and another frees him.

Core Idea
Tadbir is divisible like freeing; compensation follows loss of benefits.

This lesson examines a detailed slave scenario from Kanz al-Daqa'iq involving three masters. One master makes the slave mudabbar (free upon death), another immediately frees him, and the third does nothing. Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differ on whether the tadbir is divisible, leading to different rulings on compensation. Understanding this intra-madhhab dispute clarifies the Hanafi principles of tadbir and guarantee (damān).

Guided Path
1
State the Scenario
Identify the three parties: master A makes the slave mudabbar, master B frees him, master C remains silent. The slave is owned equally.
All masters are wealthy (musar).
2
Abu Hanifa's Ruling
State that Abu Hanifa holds tadbir divisible. The silent partner (C) claims one-third of the slave's value as a slave (qinn) from the mudabbar (A). The manumitter (B) claims one-third of the slave's value as mudabbar (which is two-thirds of the full value) from the mudabbar (A).
Clarify the numerical example: total value 27 dinars, C gets 9 from A, B gets 6 from A.
3
Sahibayn's Ruling and Reasoning
The Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue tadbir is indivisible; the entire slave becomes mudabbar by the first act. Hence the manumission is void. The manumitter (B) must compensate both partners for their shares (two-thirds of the slave's value).
They compare tadbir to full manumission which is indivisible.
4
Analyze the Qiyas (Analogy)
Abu Hanifa uses qiyas khafi: tadbir is like partial freeing (itlaf of benefits), divisible and compensable per share. The Sahibayn consider it closer to full freedom, thus indivisible.
The illa (cause) is the effect on benefits: sale is lost by tadbir, which is compensable.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, both acts are effective; under Sahibayn, only the tadbir is valid. The fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view.
Thus the mufta bihi is that the silent partner claims from the mudabbar, and the manumitter gets compensation from the mudabbar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبد الموسرين [بكسر الراء أي مملوك لهم وهم ثلاثة. (ط عيني)] صورة المسألة عبد بين ثلاثة نفر موسرين: دبره واحد وحرره ،آخر وسكت الثالث فالحكم فيه أن يأخذ الساكت الضمان من المدبر ثلث قيمته يأخذ المعتق ثلث قيمته حال كونه مدبراً، وليس له أي للساكت أن يضمن المعتق؛ لأن الأصل في الضمان أن من يكون ضمان معاوضة، وهذا إنما يتحقق في تضمين المدبر ؛ لكونه قابلاً للنقل من ملك إلى ملك وقت التدبير؛ لكونه قناً عند ذلك، ولا يمكن ذلك في الإعتاق لأجل التدبير؛ لأنه لا يقبل النقل من ملك إلى ملك، فكان قنا، والمدبر ضمان فيه ضمان إتلاف، لكن المدبر كان متمكناً قبل عتقه من استخدامه وإجارته وإعارته إلى موته. فامتنع بعتقه كل ذلك، وهذا معنى الإتلاف، وإنما يأخذ الساكت من المدبر ثلث قيمته قناً؛ لأن المنافع ثلاثة أنواع: الوطء والاستخدام والبيع، فبالتدبير فات البيع، والمدبر يأخذ من المعتق ثلث العبد مدبراً لا ما ضمن وهو ثلث قيمته قناً، توضيحه أن قيمة العبد إذا كانت سبعة وعشرين دينارا مثلا، فإن الساكت يضمن المدبر تسعة، والمدبر يضمن ستة؛ لأن قيمة المدبر ثلثا قيمة القن، وبالتدبير تلفت منه تسعة، وكان الإتلاف بالإعتاق واقعاً على قيمة المدبر وهي ثلثا قيمة القن وهي ثمانية عشر وثلثها ستة فيضمن المدبر المعتق تلك الستة فقط، ولا يضمنه الساكت. التسعة التي . وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : العبد كله صار مديراً للذي دبره أول مرة وإعتاق المعتق باطل، ويضمن لشريكيه ثلثي قيمته موسراً كان أو معسراً، وأصله أن التدبير يتجزأ عنده كالعتق بمعنى أنه إزالة الملك، وعندهما: لا يتجزأ التدبير؛ لأنه شعبة من الإعتاق وموجبه تحقق الحرية، فيكون معتبراً بحقيقة الحرية. (عيني، مستخلص، فتح) الساكت: أي الذي لم يفعل شيئاً أي يأخذ الضمان. (عيني) المدبر : بكسر الباء ثلث قيمته قناً ولا يضمن المعتق. (ط، عيني) والمدبر : أي ضمن المدبر بكسر الراء أيضاً. (عيني) مدبراً : أي حال كونه مدبّرا بفتح الباء. (عيني) لا ما ضمن أي لا يضمن المدبر المعتق الثلث الذي ضمنه للساكت. . (عيني)
The slave of the wealthy: the picture of the issue is a slave among three wealthy persons: one made him mudabbar, another freed him, and the third remained silent. The ruling is that the silent one takes guarantee from the mudabbar: one third of his value as a slave. The manumitter takes one third of his value in the state of being mudabbar. It is not permissible for the silent one to guarantee the manumitter, because the origin of guarantee is that it is a guarantee of exchange, and this only applies in the guarantee of the mudabbar because he is transferable from ownership to ownership at the time of tadbir because he is a slave then. That is not possible in manumission because of tadbir; because it does not accept transfer from ownership to ownership, so he was a slave, and the mudabbar is a guarantee of destruction. But the mudabbar was able before his freeing to use him, hire him, lend him until death. So his freeing prevented all that, and this is the meaning of destruction. The silent one only takes from the mudabbar one third of his value as a slave, because the benefits are three types: intercourse, use, and sale. By tadbir, sale is lost. The mudabbar takes from the manumitter one third of the slave as mudabbar, not what was guaranteed, which is one third of his value as a slave. Clarification: if the slave's value is 27 dinars, the silent one guarantees the mudabbar 9, and the mudabbar guarantees 6; because the value of the mudabbar is two-thirds of the slave's value, and by tadbir 9 is lost. The destruction caused by manumission falls on the value of the mudabbar, which is 18, and one third of that is 6, so the mudabbar guarantees the manumitter only those 6, and the silent one does not guarantee him the 9. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The Two said: the whole slave becomes mudabbar for the first who made him mudabbar, and the manumitter's freeing is void, and he guarantees his two partners two-thirds of his value, whether wealthy or poor. The basis is that tadbir is divisible according to Abu Hanifa like freeing in the sense of removing ownership, but according to the Two, tadbir is not divisible because it is a branch of freeing and its effect is actual freedom, so it is considered in terms of actual freedom.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage details the case of a slave owned equally by three wealthy masters. One master makes the slave mudabbar (meaning he will be free upon the master's death). Another master immediately frees the slave (manumission). The third master does nothing. According to Abu Hanifa, the silent master can claim compensation from the mudabbar (the one who made the slave mudabbar) for the value of his share (one-third of the slave's value as an ordinary slave). The manumitter, however, must pay the mudabbar one-third of the slave's value in his state as mudabbar (which is two-thirds of the ordinary value). The reasoning is that tadbir causes a loss of the ability to sell, which is one of the benefits, and this loss is compensated. Abu Hanifa also holds that tadbir is divisible, so the mudabbar validly made his share mudabbar while the manumitter's freeing of his share is effective on that share only. The Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that tadbir is not divisible; the entire slave becomes mudabbar after the first act, so the later manumission is void, and the one who attempted to free must compensate his partners. The calculation example using 27 dinars illustrates the distribution of compensation.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): The act of making a slave free upon the owner's death (mudabbar).
مستولد (mustawlada): A female slave who has borne a child to her master (umm walad).
ضمان (damān): Guarantee or compensation for loss.
إتلاف (itlāf): Destruction or loss caused by an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does the silent partner claim from the mudabbar and not the manumitter?
A) Because the mudabbar is wealthier
B) Because the mudabbar caused the loss of the ability to sell, which is a compensable benefit
C) Because the manumitter is not liable for any loss
D) Because the silent partner has no claim against anyone

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, tadbir is divisible, and compensation among partners follows the loss of benefits per share.

Father Buying His Son: Guarantee for the Seller
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee when father buys son

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on guarantee when a father buys his son and explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: no guarantee because seller shared in the cause; Sahibayn: guarantee if father is wealthy.

This lesson examines a specific case from the fiqh of sales: a father purchasing his own son from a seller. The ruling is not uniform within the Hanafi school; Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differ on whether the father owes a guarantee (ḍamān) to the seller. The passage also presents a consensus case and an analogous scenario involving an umm walad, helping you see how the underlying principle is applied consistently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the core dispute
Read the first part of the passage: a father buys his son from a seller who owns the entire son. Note that Abu Hanifa says no guarantee, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say guarantee is wajib if the father is wealthy.
The seller is a third party, not a co-owner.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa's basis: the seller 'shared in the cause' (al-illa huwa al-bay'). Because the seller participated in the sale transaction, he cannot claim compensation. Understand this as a principle: one who voluntarily enters a transaction cannot later seek damages from the other party.
Cause here refers to the sale itself as the operative cause of any potential guarantee.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's reasoning
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) condition guarantee on the father's wealth. They analogize to the case of buying from a partner, where there is consensus on guarantee if the father is wealthy.
Their dalil is based on the partner case, implying that wealth triggers liability.
4
Examine the consensus case and the analogy
The passage states that if the father buys from one of two partners, and he is wealthy, guarantee is due by consensus. Then it gives an analogous case: if instead of a son there is a jointly owned umm walad, and the husband acquires her, he must guarantee half to his partner regardless of how ownership was acquired.
This shows consistent application: when the seller is a co-owner (partner), guarantee is always due, unlike when the seller is a full owner.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The difference matters in court: if a father buys his son, the seller cannot claim compensation under Abu Hanifa's view. Under the Sahibayn's view, a wealthy father must pay. Most Hanafi practice follows Abu Hanifa.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's position, as Kanz al-Daqaiq typically favors his view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اشترى نصف ابنه ممن يملك كله لا يضمن لبائعه شيئاً عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن البائع شاركه في العلة وهو البيع، وقالا : إن كان الأب موسراً يجب عليه الضمان ولو اشتراه أبوه من أحد الشريكين، وهو موسر لزمه الضمان بالإجماع، ولو كان مكان الابن جارية مستولدة بالنكاح فملكها الزوج مع غيره يجب عليه ضمان النصف لشريكه كيف ما كان وإن كانا ملكاها بإرث . [رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۸/۱] (مستخلص)
And if he buys half of his son from someone who owns the whole, he does not guarantee anything to his seller according to Abu Hanifa, because the seller shared with him in the cause, which is the sale. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If the father is wealthy, guarantee is obligatory upon him. And if a father buys him from one of the two partners while he is wealthy, guarantee is obligatory by consensus. And if instead of the son there was a slave-girl who is umm walad through marriage, and the husband owns her along with another, then he must guarantee half to his partner in all cases, even if they inherited her.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents the first mas'alah of the chapter: a father who purchases his son from a seller who owned the entire son. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the father owes no guarantee (damān) to the seller because the seller is considered to have shared in the cause of the transaction (the sale) and thus is not entitled to compensation. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that if the father is wealthy, he must guarantee the seller. The text also notes a case where the father buys from one of two partners: there is consensus that if the father is wealthy, guarantee is due. An analogous case is mentioned: if the son is replaced by a concubine (umm walad) owned jointly by the husband and another, and the husband acquires her, he must guarantee half to his partner regardless of how they came to own her, even by inheritance. This shows the difference in the scope of guarantee rules.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Compensation or guarantee for loss; here specifically monetary liability for the value of the slave when sold.
موسر (mūsir): Wealthy, having sufficient means to pay compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does a father who buys his son from a seller not owe guarantee?
A) Because the father owns the son by nature
B) Because the seller shared in the cause (the sale)
C) Because the son is not property
D) Because the father is poor

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa holds no guarantee when a father buys his son from a sole seller because the seller shared in the cause of the sale; the Sahibayn condition guarantee on the father's wealth.

Claim of Umm Walad Between Partners: Service and Earnings
Intra-Hanafi dispute on joint slave-girl claimed as umm walad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position and the Sahibayn position on the treatment of a jointly owned slave-girl claimed as umm walad, and explain why Abu Hanifa's view became mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Certainty of ownership preserves half rights; the other half is suspended.

This lesson examines a dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples (the Sahibayn) about the status of a jointly owned slave-girl when one partner claims she is the umm walad of the other. The ruling turns on the principle of certainty (yaqin) versus doubt (shakk) in ownership. We will walk through the arguments and see why Abu Hanifa's view ultimately prevailed.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the key facts: two partners jointly own a slave-girl; one partner claims she is the other's umm walad; the other denies.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa: she serves the denying partner half the time and works for her own keep the other half; no sa'yah.
3
Explain the basis
Half ownership is certain (since the denier certainly owns half); the other half is doubtful, so it is suspended.
This is the key usul principle.
4
Identify the Sahibayn's ruling
The Sahibayn: the denying partner cannot use her service but can compel her to perform sa'yah for half her value, after which she is free.
5
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the girl remains in limbo; under the Sahibayn, she can earn her freedom.
6
Note the tarjih
Abu Yusuf later retracted to Abu Hanifa's view, making it the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لشريكه : هي أم ولدك وأنكر، تخدمه يوماً وتتوقف يوماً، وما لأم ولد أي رجل في الأمة أي الأمة أي تخدم الأمة المنكر بلا خدمة ونفقها في كسبها تقوم، فلا يضمن أحد الشريكين بإعتاقها له ثلاثة أعبد قال لاثنين أحدكما حر، فخرج واحد ودخل آخر، وكرر ومات بلا بيان منهما الثالث أي وإن مات المولى وأنكر : أي الشريك ذلك فهي موقوفة. (عيني) وتتوقف يوماً : أي إذا كانت جارية بين الشريكين فقال أحدهما لصاحبه: هي أم ولدك، فأنكر صاحبه فهي تخدم لشريكه المنكر يوما وتتوقف يوماً فتكتسب فيه ما تنفق عليها ولا سعاية عليها للمقر عند أبي حنيفة، ونصف كسبها للمنكر وإن لم يكن لها كسب فنصف نفقتها على المنكر؛ لأن نصفها للمنكر بيقين؛ لأن المقر إن كان صادقاً فهي أم ولده، وإن كان كاذباً فهي مشتركة بينهما، فكان نصف الخدمة مستحقاً له، ووقع الاشتباه في النصف الآخر فيتوقف، وقالا: ليس للمنكر أن يستخدمها، وله أن يستسعيها في نصف قيمتها، ثم تكون حرة، ولا سبيل عليها لأحد؛ لأن المقر أفسد نصيبه ونصيب الشريك، وتعذر التضمين؛ لعدم البيئة، فوجبت السعاية عليها، وبه قالت الثلاثة، وذكر في "الأصل" رجوع أبي يوسف إلى قول أبي حنيفة رمز الحقائق: ۳۲۸/۱] (مستخلص)
And if he says to his partner: 'She is your umm walad' and he denies, she serves him one day and stops one day. And 'what is for umm walad?' i.e., any man (has no value) regarding the slave-girl. i.e., the slave-girl serves the denying partner without compensation and her maintenance is from her earnings. So neither partner is liable for freeing her. [Then a different case:] He said to two (slaves): 'One of you is free,' and one left and another entered, and he repeated (the statement) and died without clarification from them; the third (case): if the master dies and the partner denies. i.e., the partner denies, then she is suspended. Explanation: If there is a slave-girl between two partners, and one says to his partner: 'She is your umm walad,' and the partner denies, then she serves the denying partner one day and stops one day, working to earn her maintenance. There is no sa'yah upon her for the affirming partner according to Abu Hanifa. Half of her earnings belong to the denying partner; if she has no earnings, half of her maintenance is on the denying partner, because half of her certainly belongs to the denying partner. For if the affirming partner is truthful, she is his umm walad; if he is lying, she is shared between them. So half of the service is due to him, and the other half is doubtful, so it is suspended. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The denying partner may not use her services, but he may compel her to work for half her value, after which she becomes free, and no one has any claim on her. Because the affirming partner corrupted his share and the partner's share, and compensation is impossible for lack of evidence, so sa'yah is obligatory upon her. And the three (i.e., the three imams) said this. And it is mentioned in al-Asl that Abu Yusuf retracted to Abu Hanifa's opinion. [Reference: Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/328]
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage deals with a slave-girl jointly owned by two partners. One partner claims that she is the umm walad of the other partner (i.e., she has borne a child to him). If the other partner denies this, there is a dispute about how the girl should be treated. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the girl serves the denying partner one day and is free the next day to work for her own upkeep. The denying partner gets half her earnings because his ownership of half is certain. The affirming partner cannot claim service or sa'yah. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani disagree: they rule that the denying partner cannot use her services but can require her to work to free herself by paying half her value. The text notes that in al-Asl, Abu Yusuf eventually retracted to Abu Hanifa's view. This shows the ijtihadi nature of the ruling and the importance of certainty in ownership.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has born a child to her master; she becomes free upon his death.
سعاية (sa'yah): Earning by a slave or partially freed person to pay for freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what happens to the jointly owned slave-girl when one partner claims she is the other's umm walad and the other denies?
A) She serves the denying partner half-time.
B) She must perform sa'yah for half her value then becomes free.
C) She is freed immediately.
D) She remains suspended indefinitely.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, certainty of ownership prevails over doubt, and intra-madhhab ikhtilaf is resolved by later consensus, as seen in Abu Yusuf's retraction.
